MEDLOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform aln 08b
adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminei tly
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to h, ve
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PAIiK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKEu
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large -from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
' The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MEDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
.Years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-

SATURDAY, AUGUST 8, 1891.
“Great Parkes": Is Parkes Great?
When that small individual, the Honour-
able Daniel O’Connor, Esquire, M.L.C.,
N S. W, Postmaster - General (we spare
him not one tittle of his official marks
of insignificance), bestowed the name of
the “Great Parses” upon a large noise-
making machine, hoisted to an elevated
position, the act was a mere dribble of
adulation from an obsequious mind. Your
toadies are, ofteuer than not, prodigiously
dull fellows, and as blind to the ridiculous
aspect as they are keen-sighted in regard to
the profitable side of grovelling. It is probably
due to this defect of vision that the bell
which the escaped N.S. W. Postmaster-
General has ordained shall be known as
“ Great Parkes,” bears somewhere stamped
on it the name of the O’Connor. In this
adjustment there are evidences of the inner
workings of the O'Connor mind. His hope
of fame is founded —brass-founded —on ring-
ing down the long-resounding aisles of time,
amidst the vibrations caused by “ Great
Parkes.’ When, in the future, some country-
man, dinned by the noise of the tolling, is
informed that it is “ Great Parkes ’
which clatters, and after inquiring whether
it had for godfather the National Park or
Moore Park, climbs up to have a look at the
car-torturing boll, he will perceive, by read-
ing the legend cast upon if, that there was
once a person named O’Connor, who was
head-postman at a time when there was a
parish or something of the kind known as
New South Wales. It is unlikely that
curiosity will be pushed further. The dull
bucolic will not pursue his investigations to
ascertain, after infinite research, that the
prehistoric postman amused a bygone
generation by bellowing, with empurpled
countenance, unintelligible rhapsodies on
post-prandial occasions, and mouthing ex-
cruciatingly funny oiations, studded with
screamingly pretentious sentences in, to
himself, unknown tongues, while expanding
his letter-slit at the reception of persons
distinguished among oars. To become alle-
gorical, who on this globe cares to know the
name, the breed, or the colour, of any one
among the dogs which licked the hand of
Dives, or the sores of Lazarus 1
• • •
It appears unlikely that the craving after
immortal fame will bo gratified to any person
who founds his expectations upon the cir-
cumstance that ho is subjected to disturb-
ance whenever Parkes scratches himself.
There is even room for doubt whether that
historic renown which Sir Henry Parkes
himself openly aspires to win, will reward
his strenuous exertions. The question
whether Parkes is a great man, as Mr.
O’Connor would seem to signify that he is,
by the baptismal name he has conferred
upon seven tons of metal with its tongue in its
cheek, appears worthy of consideration. In
order to clear the way, it is needful to arrive
at an understanding as to what is signified
by the term “a great man.” We might
otherwise be encountered at the outset, or
confounded in the end, by some moral con-
tention that greatness and goodness are in-
separable, and that as Sir Henry Parkes
is the embodiment of all the private
virtues, he must be great ; or, on the
other hand, by the baseless assertion
that inasmuch as he is chargeable with
almost every vice which disfigures a character,
he must be small. Nothing is more hopeless
than an attempt to define what constitutes
greatness in a man. Essentially it must
signify the possession of natural powers or
qualities which, when brought into action,
bring their possessor into' eminence. This
is merely a periphrasis, signifying that a
great man is a man who possesses genius and
has had opportunity for employing it. But
what is genius' The author of “Lacon”
says, correctly enough, we fancy, that “the
plain unvarnished fact, after all- that has
been said about it, is this: that genius, in one
grand particular, is like life. Wo know
nothing of either, but by their effects.”
# # #
If this be accepted as correct, the great-
ness of men must be measured by results.
And results are dependent upon causes. The
greatest of cows was the one which kicked
over a lanthorn and thereby started the con-
flagration which destroyed London. But, for
the achievement of fame, accessory condi-
ditions were necessary to this cow. Plenty
of cows have kicked as hard, but only to
this one did Fate ordain a lanthorn, a truss
of straw, a stall like tinder, and a wooden
city. Jonathan Wilde appears, from what
can be learned of him, to have had genius
quite equal to that possessed by Fouche, yet
Wilde lived by blackmailing thieves and
harlots and was hanged, ivhereas Fouche,
with a revolution-to work on, blackmailed
bankers and princes, and died a Duke. The
best way to arrive at an idea of what is styled
greatness in men is to consider eminent
examples. And when this is done, it becomes
clear that all idea of associating goodness
with greatness must be abandoned. Indeed,
so far as this popular conception of greatness
is concerned, it has to be confessed that
greatness and goodness are incompatible.
Alexander the Great would never have been
heard of after his own century had he not
been a thoroughly reckless and inhuman
brute. Caesar’s greatest repute is due to
his intended treason against his country.
Peter of Russia, Frederick of Prussia, how
much less great would not they have been
had they been amiable characters ? Marl-
rorough might have lived and died a led
captain in the train of some rich noble had
he been too lofty-minded to profit by
the prostitution of himself to Charles ll. ’s
mistress, and of Ids sister to James 11., and
would have died in poverty and exile had he
not betrayed James to William, Napoleon
Buonaparte might have missed the chances
which carried him to Empire had he been
too nice to accept a bride from the soiling
hand of Barras. Cromwell could never
have been Lord Protector, despite his
military talents, had be not condescended to
cant through his nose. The virtuous patriot,
Algernon Sydney, would probably never
have had the chance of exhibiting his virtue
and patriotism had he not kept up his position
by—living on bribes from the King of
France. Very likely there existed beside
him in public affairs some rival patriot who
sank out of notice because he wouldn’t take
bribes, and so was economising in the
country while Sydney was flaunting hisvirtue
in the metropolis.
• • •
It would he no sufficient retort against
any declaration that Parkes is a great
man, to allege that he has been or is a
bad one. A pure man might attain great-
ness in Heaven. Such a one might oven
become groat in learning or in scientific
circles. Ordinarily, however, the most
learned or the most gifted in science, would
not bo those whom the world esteemed
highest. Professor Holloway was, on the
whole, more widely celebrated than Pro-
fessor Simpson, and the pills and ointment
tau?ht that “ the blood is the life,” in
central China, for instance, where the virtues
of chloroform were unknown. To argue
that Parkes is a quack—an advertising
politician—as Raikes, the British Post-
master-General called Henniker-Heaton,
is of no avail. It may be admitted that
Parkes opens correspondence with all sorts
of celebrated persons, and when he receives
a dose of flattery in return for an application
of flummery, he invariably sends it to the
newspapers so that the vulgar crowd may
bo duly impressed with the idea that Mr.
Lecky, or Sir Charles Dilke, holds him in
the highest esteem and consideration. This
“bold advertisement” is a recognised
method of great men. Bucke, in his Book
of Human Character, treats of this pecu-
liarity. “In all stations,” ho remarks,
“supported by the public, nothing succeeds
so amply and so surely as quackery judi-
ciously exercised. Even groat men resort to
it.” A writer in the Edinburgh Review
expressed the same opinion in 1828.
Thus:—“We often feel tempted to say, in
these disingenuous days, when we have been
in company with any distinguished per-
son—-ho is a great man, no doubt, but
he is, evidently, a great quack also.” Conse-
quently, even although Sir Henry Parkes
stood convicted of being a stupendous hum-
bug in the matter of self-advertising, that
would be no bar to his being ranked as a
great man. Lord Bacon affirms that Socra-
tes, Aristotle, Galen, were men full of
ostentation, and affirms that vain glory
lielpeth to perpetuate a man’s memory. The
same philosophic observer mentions that
Tacitus attributed to Muctanus ' hat, by a
certain art, he made a display of all he had
said or done, which seems equally applicable
to Parkes. Certainly, we do not know who
Mucianus was, but probably he was reckoned
a considerable man in the time of Tacitus,
else the latter would scarcely have alleged
him as an example. Since, however, the
fame of Mucianus has died away, it may be
inferred that quackery will not suffice to
preserve the celebrity of a second or third-
rate man. This may, perhaps, be regarded
as discouraging by the sponsor of “Great
Parkes ”
# # #
It will not be sufficient for those who
would seek to deny the attribute of great-
ness to Parkes, to allege that he has been
gu’lty of actions infinitely mean or even
atrocious. For instance, that somewhat
notorious prison episode concerning which
he was reproached many years ago,
had an almost exact parallel in ancient
times. Dionysius, the bloody tyrant of
Syracuse, was a great monarch, yet he was
the inventor and enjoyer of that dungeon of
peculiar construction known as Dionysius’
ear, which was so planned that a funnel con-
veyed to an aperture in the tyrant’s chamber
every word which passed between the
prisoners incarcerated, and made their royal
gaoler the recipient of their most sacred con-
fidences. Nor will it serve to allege against
the candidate for greatness instances of
miserable malignity or pettiness. It is
surely undeniable that Napoleon I. was one
of the world’s greatest men Yet how
wretchedly small was his persecution of
Madame de Stael ! It is necessary to strike
an average. The incongruous composition of
human nature perpetually puzzles. Take
that same Napoleon, for example, the hero
of the Bridge of Lodi, the undaunted par-
ticipant in a hundred pitched battles, un-
moved by showers of shot and shell It was
the same man who turned white and nearly
fainted on the shoulder of a grenadier when,
in the Council of Five Hundred, some
wrathful members drew daggers ; the same
hero who, after the Fontainebleau abdication,
quivered and wept with apprehension of
personal violence while on his route through
Southern France to embark for Elba. Dr.
Johnson appreciated these variations and
apparently irreconcilable qualities in the
human mind when he wrote the line
Fears of the brave, and follies of the wise.
The last phrase will serve to confound those
detractors who would fain assure us that
Parkes is altogether devoid of talent, be-
cause he is author of such idiocies as the pro-
posal to change the name “ New South
Wales ” to “ Australia,” and of the ridiculous
“ Dead-house ” proposals.
* * *
Although it has been shown that greatness,
in the ordinary sense of the word as applied to
individuals, is usually inseparable from crime,
it would not be just to deny the attribute of
greatness to Parkes, merely because he is
accustomed to assert in the most emphatic
manner, and is supported by quite a crowd
of sycophants in that assertion (which wo
do not propose to discuss), that ho has
never departed from the paths of public
rectitude and virtue and is innocent of
any ill-deed. A man may have been
guilty of everything which is requisite
to put the stamp of greatness upon
his career, and yet may assert with
the most ingenuous effrontery that his
life has been unsullied by a single act which
he should regret. Apart from the slaughter
of over a million of men, immolated in wars
of which he was the originator, Napoleon
committed many acts which might surely have
weighed on his conscience. But, no ! After
all was past, he asked O’Meara, in St.
Helena, “ What reward have not 1 to
expect —I, who have run a career so extraor-
dinary, so tempestuous, without committing
a single crime ? ” This self-satisfaction is
much of a piece with the letters of Pious
Billy, late Emperor of Germany, to dear
Augusta, the wife whom he neglected for
pots of the ballet. During the war with
France, his letters were full of slaughter and
religious sentiment in about equal propor-
tions, and their tone has been epigram ma-
tised somewhat thus
Webe sent ten thousand French below,
Praise God from whom all blessings flow.
• # •
Parkes has never soared to quite so lofty
a pitch of greatness as that, but apparently
ho has alit on a lower branch of splendour
only because his chances have been smaller.
His despatch of troops with gatling guns to
Newcastle was quite in tho correct spirit, and
no sensible person would hesitate to believe
that he would have been equally prompt to
set a million men in motion to cross a
frontier, had he been a Minister in Prussia
or Franco. The qualities of a great man
were exhibited ; only the scope of action
and the scale of materials wore- limited. As
Grey says, his “lot forbade” splendid deeds
on a magnificent icale :
“ Nor circumscribed alone
Their growing virtues, hut their crimes confined ;
Forbade to wade thro’ slaughter to a Throne,
Or shut the gates of mercy on mankind."
This accidental drop into poetry reminds us
that Parkes lias been the author of some
peculiai’ly atrocious verses, and lias not even
evinced sufficient literary sense to come in
out of the rain when it showered critics.
His detractors allege that no man but a fool
could have published such flagrant evidences
of imbecility as Parke-5 has thrust under the
very eyes of people in the shape of maudlin
love-poems, and rickety sonnets. But those
who thus argue have cither not known, or
have forgotten, that Richelieu, who was
undeniably a very great man, exhibited pre-
cisely the same weakness, and exposed him-
self to the covert derision of his contempo-
raries by insisting on the public performance
of a series of dramas composed by himself,
of the most abject literary quality.
* * «
It will not bo sufficient even to demonstrate
that Parkes, during all his years of political
power, has never accomplished one consider-
able act. off his own bat, so to speak. It
may be true that all the enactments which
ho claims the credit of having placed on the
Statute-book wore really embodiments of
principles which he had opposed, and which
were conceived or battled for by other men,
until the public appreciated their value,
whereupon Parkes “conveyed” them and
patented them in his own name. But,
even supposing this Were so, and
everything which he attempted on his own
account was either a farce or a fiasco, no one
can deny that he has kept in office longer
than any other man. And this is as much
as M azarin managed to effect, all hough
Mazarin’s greatness is scarcely contested.
Only Mazarin made better financial weather
of it and escaped with some seven millions
sterling, whereas Parkes has had to be
satisfied with failing for less than a seventieth
of the amount. In fine, Parkes may
not be a great man : but he has many of the
worst symptoms of the disease. We defy
anyone to say that we have not held the
balance fairly in weighing his merits in the
foregoing article, and just to sum up and to
put the question entirely beyond doubt, we
shall conclude with a couple of quotations
from “ Lacon ” : “Great men, like
comets, are eccentric in their courses,
and formed to do extensive good, by
methods unintelligible to vulgar minds.”
Again : “Great men often obtain their ends
by means beyond the grasp of vulgar intel-
lects, and even by methods diametrically
opposite to those which the multitude would
pursue. But to effect this, bespeaks as
profound a knowledge of mind as that
philosopher evinced of matter who first
produced ice by the agency of heat.” And
now, if there remains doubt in the mind of
any reader as to whether Parkes be a great
man or not, it is no fault of ours. We are
confident we have made the matter clear
enough. We admit, of course, that we are
a great man ourselves, and that our means
of exposition may be “ beyond the grasp of
vulgar intellects, and our methods' diame-
trically opposite to those which the multi-
tude would pursue.” But we can’t help
that. We must submit to pay the penalty
of greatness.
The Anglo-Saxon “Destiny.”
The London Spectator, in a recent issue,
revives the old impossible theory of a great
federation of all the English-speaking races,
and in dealing with this subject it incidentally
fixes up a new destiny for Australia. There is
nothing surprising about this, for the British
journalist is perpetually arranging somebody’s
destiny, and in a general way he keeps his eye
on the earth and the heavens, and fastens up
the solar system when it chances to get loose.
Nothing ever comes of any of his theories, but
while they last they furnish amusement for this
old, tired globe ; and none of them ever do
much harm, for the human race drifts along
just the same. Possibly, after all, the world
hasn’t got any destiny in particular, and for
that reason, it may be, the plans of the news-
paper philanthropist fall flat. In the English-
man’s superlative egotism, it is an article of
faith with him that some day there will be
some great consummation of the Anglo-Saxon
races, when they will all unite together and do
something, or leave something undone, or when
they will be something, or else will give up
being something that they used to be ; and it
is presumed that all the scattered of the race
are imperceptibly moving towards this focus.
The dead Imperial Federation fad was a part of
this great scheme. The shreds and fragments
of the British empire wore all to join in forming
an immense Parliament, which would serve to
warm the inmost soul of every widely-severed
Briton when he looked at this monument of the
national greatness. It was considered by many
eminent authorities that even the humblest of
Victoria’s subjects would feel better when
there was a Federal Legislature, and would
throw out his chest further, and experience an
honest glow in the region of the stomach. And
now that Imperial Federation is only a damp,
unpleasant “ remains,” the Spectator advances
a new proposition. Instead of Federation there
is to be disintegration. Australia, Canada, the
provinces of the Cape of Good Hope, and the
other great “ dependencies ” of the Crown, are
to be transmuted into independent States ; and
then, when there are about half-a-hundred
separate Anglo-Saxon nations scattered all over
the globe, they are to be formed into a great
League of Peace, which will boss the whole
world into tranquility, and abolish war and
strife and ill-feeling for evermore.
# • #
Apparently the British newspaper is quite
serious. The great Anglo-Saxon League is to
be a perpetual brotherhood, and its mission in
the future will be to sit down hard on every
State which wants to fight with any other
State, and to send an ultimatum to everybody,
and suppress anything. Britain, Canada, the
United States, and Australia are to be the chief
component parts of the alliance, and it is taken
for granted, as a self-obvious assumption, that
they will all gladly enter into the coining Con-
federation, and regard it as a joy and a privilege
to do so. But apparently it never comes mte
the calculation that not one of these ooinpsnen


“What are you going to do with the
farmers’ surplus corn when the distilleries are
shut up?” “We purpose to raise more hogs and
less hell.” — Amer.
A certain N.S.W. M.L.A., who has
dabbled in journalism in an amateur sort of way,
meditates starting an “ anti-shootemdown ” paper
in Melbourne, and with that view seeks to enlist
the literary services of A. G. Taylor.
mrts really cates a straw for any peace save its
()W n or that not one of them, excepting Eng-
lnul’ itself, takes the slightest interest in the
destiny of the great Anglo-Saxon race. If that
rxec has a destiny—apart from the inevitable
business of living and growing old—the fact
will doubtless be discovered in course ot time,
but many a nation seems to have none at all,
•md quite possibly the British people have
already developed all the destiny they are ever
oing to have. The world is full of nations who
—except in times of war and revolution—seem
to liave no particular mission except to get up
in the morning, and wash, and make money,
and go to bed again in the evening. The
patriotic Briton, however, always assumes that
there is, in addition to all this, some hazy and
indefinite culmination ahead, when his race will
vet up and do something which it never did
f e fo r e, and when it does this thing humanity at
large will be greatly benefited thereby. As, how-
ler about the only thing which the British
nation or any other nation, has ever done that
has not been actually injurious to Humanity was
to get up and wash on a larger scale than before,
ami to go to bed earlier than usual, it is diffi-
cult to imagine what the culminator can con-
sist of The dead theory of Imperial Federa-
tion and the still-born theory of an Anglo-
Saxon League of Peace were both supported by
many arguments, all of them as indefinite as a
spectre in a fog, about the mission of the
English-speaking peoples, and the great work
that lay before that mighty nation ; and both
these schemes were foreordained to fall flat
because nobody ever defined what that mission
or that work amounted to.
# * •
The supposition that the future Australian
Republic will ever join in a League for the
maintenance of universal peace is an absurdity
of which only a British theorist could be guilty.
The one thing which the British Tory is utterly
unable to comprehend is that Australia’s one
ambition is to stand out of all foreign complica-
tions, and that when once it starts its new
career it never wants to see nor hear—in its poli-
tical capacity—of the other branches of the
Anglo-Saxon people from that date right on to
the Day of Judgment. Neither does it want to
hear, in a political sense, of any other people.
It does not even desire that its foreign relations
should be of a friendly character—what it longs
for is to have no foreign relations at all. If the
outside world is at peace it will be glad to hear
of the fact, and if the outside world is at war it
may, or it may not, regret the circumstance.
That is to say, it will regret it just as much as
any other nation might do, but it is more than
questionable if any nation ever does feel
seriously grieved about a war in which it is
not personally concerned, and the majority of
mankind rather enjoy battles, murders, con-
flagrations, and sudden deaths, so long as they
remain at a safe distance. Therefore a League
of Peace is merely a scheme to secure peace to
the members of the League, and any member of
the proposed League which has got peace
already will naturally stand out. And as a
still more sufficient reason why it will stand
out, the League of Peace would be just a shade
more disastrous than a league for the promotion
of universal war. The only method by which
any confederation could promote general tran-
quility would be by taking a hand in every war
that arises, and assisting to pound the aggres-
sor ; and when he was sufficiently pounded the
moral principles of the League would forbid it
to take a share of the plunder. By the time
the united English-speaking races had knocked
out every military Power on earth, the great
principle of brotherhood might possibly begin
to make some progress, but in the meantime
the League would have to maintain the biggest
army and navy, and the largest war-budget,
known in all history. This does not read like
an attractive programme; and if the vaunted
destiny of the English people is to promote the
cause of world-wide peace, it would be cheaper
and more peaceful to have no destiny at all.
• * •
There is no doubt, however, that Britain does
long with a great longing, for an era of universal
tranquillity. There is no particular Christian
sentiment in this yearning, but at all events it
rests on a solid cash basis. Britain has lent
money to every Power of any fighting capacity
on earth, and every new war threatens to send
a long list of her hoarded millions to that
Gehenna which is paved with good intentions
and bad debentures. If she goes to war, it is
ten chances to one that her enemy is fighting
her with her own money. If she wins, she pro-
bably has to lend her opponent the cash to pay
the indemnity, and never gets it back. If two
other nations fight, it often happens that they
are both doing it with British cash, and then
there are two debtors being crippled instead of
one. Consequently Britain has abundant reason
to desire a long period of rest. But Australia,
Canada, and the United States have no wars on
hand nor any prospects of war, neither have
they lent any money for other nations to fire
away in gunpowder ; therefore, they have no
desire to bounce the world at great expense
into tranquillity, in order that Britain’s debtors
may have an opportunity to recuperate. The
British idea of an Australian Republic is
peculiar. To the Englishman, Australia still
comes under the heading of “ our colonies,” and
is spoken of as a “ possession.” If it ever gets
independence— a matter which is still regarded
as more than questionable—that boon is to be
‘ granted ” upon some such condition as that of
an eternal alliance, offensive and defensive—
some such alliance as the Spectator’s fanciful
League of Peace; but the majority of English
politicians still hanker after Imperial Federa-
tion as a preferable measure, and do not know
that their fad is dead and buried, and gone to
impalpable dust. And the fact that Australia
wants no Federation, no offensive nor defensive
alliance, no bund nor zollverein nor commercial
treaty, is a matter which the British intelligence
refuses to take in.
» * *
And the most incomprehensible thing of all
to the British patriot is that Australia should
wantonly throw away the “ traditions of the
Empire,” and should unconcernedly abandon
its share in the alleged glorious destiny
°f Britain. It is these traditions and
that aforesaid other article which make
it impossible for the Englishman to believe
in the reality of Australian Republicanism.
The memories of the old boiler-plated warriors
who went out amid mud and slush and thunder
to stick forks and props into each other at
Crecy—the recollections of the Ironsides who
snutiled nasal texts, and sold their enemies to
be slaves on the Virginian plantations —the
visions of a long line of kings, some of them
mad, all of them bad, and many of them drunken
—these are the things which the Australian
Republicans want to throw away with as little
respect as a Kurdish raider might throw away
a beadle. And not only that, but they propose
to sacrifice their share in the destiny of the
British race, which is still more unaccountable.
Probably it would be useless to explain that
this country doesn’t want to take any risks—
that most nations which have appeared in his-
tory seemed to have no destiny worth speaking
of, and those which were furnished with the
article generally had a bad one, and, therefore,
Australia desires to stand from under lest
Britain should have the much-drivelled-about
thing after all, and it should do some damage.
And if these provinces appear inclined to reck-
lessly sacrifice that other matter which is
vaguely spoken of in after-dinner speeches as
the “heritage of this great people,” it would
be of no use to explain that Australia wants a
new heritage of its own—if there is such a
thing—and not a share in an old, second-hand
article which never amounted to much even
when it was fresh. Also, it would be idle to
mention that the oft-quoted tie of a com-
mon blood is of no account ; or that the
future Australian Republic would join any
Anglo-Australian brotherhood either for the
promotion of peace or for any other purpose,
because the only way to make universal peace
is to fall heavily on any States which make war,
and that kind of peace would be too expensive.
In short, the ambition of this nation is to form
an independent State which will mind its own
business, and thus to follow the example of
Switzerland, the only Commonwealth on earth
which has consistently stayed at home and
looked after its own affairs, and never had a
“ destiny,” or a “ heritage,” or a mission, or a
K.C.M.G., or a desire to promote the peace of
the whole human race, or any other such sub-
stance. But no doubt this explanation would
fall flat. Too much history has driven the
British editor mad, and the idea of a nation
which doesn’t want to kill anybody, either in the
cause of peace or brotherhood or any other
cause whatever, is altogether beyond his com-
prehension.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Standing Still.
Following in the track of the Victorian
“ National Association,” the fatted Tories of
Queensland have resolved to strike out in the
sacred cause of Liberty, and a Patriotic League,
or something of the sort, has been called into
existence at Brisbane. Its objects are expressed
in the usual pamphlet—the same old objects
and the same old pamphlet that babble on for
ever, in every region where the Tory congre-
gates. The members of the League propose to
maintain the Constitution, whatever that may
be, and to lend a general support to the Queen ;
and they also have an eye to the moral aspect of
the case, and intend to prop up the Lord as a
respectable and well-meaning institution that
ought to be encouraged. They look with a
reproachful eye on “class legislation”—or
rather on the legislation of any class except
their own ; and they want to maintain Order—
whatever that may be also, and to preserve the
integrity of the Law. Further, the League
looks with alarm on the revolutionary ten-
dencies of a certain section of the community
which is going about like a roaring lion seeking
victuals, and it calls on all right-minded
persons, and respectable bald individuals, and
citizens with capacious stomachs, to rally up in
apromiscuous way, andmaintain sound principles
generally. Probably this poor, old, hazy associa-
tion isn’t aware of the fact, but death is written
large upon its countenance already. There is
one fatal defect in it and all similar organisa-
tions, and by reason of this defect the Queens-
land “ patriots,” and the Victorian “ National
Association,” and the British Primrose League,
and the branch Primrose League which came
out like a tired exotic in a pot, and tried to
plant itself in New South Wales, and even the
A.N.A.—unless it mends its ways —will all fade
away, and be bowled out, and will also pass in
their cheques, and be deposited in the bone-
yard of dead fads and extinct ideas. The defect
is that none of them were formed to do any-
thing. Some of them exist for the purpose of
whooping over something that was done long
ago, but the world can’t live by whoop-
ing over the past, or feed upon rockets,
and votes of confidence, and anniversaries,
and celebrations, and vociferations. Some of
them exist to prevent other people doing some-
thing, and have organised themselves for the
purpose of being an immovable stump on a plain,
but even the most immovable of stumps must
decay in the end. A few more exist simply
because they do exist, and the only cause of
their existence is that they are there, which is
exactly the same reason which leads a half-brick
to be in an uninhabited land. They are all
convinced that the destiny of man is to be as he
is now, only more so, if possible; and their
only object is to try and make the human race
more like what it is now than it is at present,
and to make each individual look more like
himself than he ever was before. And the one
thing which they utterly fail to understand is
that the world must keep doing something or
else lose its brains and its capacity altogether.
If all the world started to be a “Patriotic
League ” or a “ National Association,” it would
presently become so dense that it wouldn’t even
know hew to be an association. The one good
feature about the Primrose organisation is that
it dresses itself up in bed-furniture once a year,
and puts vegetables over a dead Hebrew'; it
would be a better feature still if it shunted trucks,
annually, or dug a hole and then filled it up
again, yet even the vegetables and the bed-
furniture represent motion in some shape. But
the Queensland “ Patriots ” and Hie Victorian
“ Nationalists” liave no dead Hebrew to travel
on, and, therefore, if the country could
exchange either of them for a shearers’ riot, or
a big street-light, or a conflagration, or a
decent-sized flood, accompanied by much loss of
property, it would make a good profit on the
transaction.
.
The Cheap Italian Labourer.
The regular yearly row about labour for the
plantations of the North has come and gone in
the Queensland Legislature. It has been
decided, as usual, that the country wants cheap
labour to “ develope its resources ” —that same
old development and the same solemn old
resources which have been trotted out so very,
very often before. It has also been decided, as
usual, that the white Australian can’t work in
that sultry land ; and, therefore, as the China-
man is prohibited, and the Coolie has been
tried, with results that were a trifle worse
than open insurrection, and the Kanaka was
abolished some time ago to make an adver-
tisement for the “Great Liberal party,” the
Griffith Government is now engaged in the im-
portation of Italians as the handiest method of
filling the gap. The men they want are the
hardy peasants of Piedmont, reared under the
shadow of the snow-clad Alps, and by some
extraordinary effort of hard lying it is assumed
that these exiles from the land of the avalanche
and the glacier are eminently fitted to fill the
bill beyond the tropic of Capricorn. In
reality, they will be found ten times more
unfitted to work under a northern sun than the
Australian himself. What Capital wants is not
tropical labour, but cheap labour, cheap
ignorance, and cheap slavery, and this whole
labour problen is based on a self-evident false-
hood. The given reason for the importation of
Chinese, Hindoos, Kanakas, ignorant low-class
Italians, and similar evils, is that the white
Australian cannot do the work required in the
cane-fields, but the white Australian can really
do any kind of work, and do it anywhere. »So,
for that matter, can the Englishman or the
Yankee; any one of the three will cheerfully
toil in the centre of Greenland, or on the Gaboon
River, or in any other spot on the
whole earth that is not absolutely pestilential,
and will tackle almost any extreme of
heat and cold that comes in his way —provided
the pay is commensurate. There is hardly a
town of any size in Britain, America, or Aus-
tralia which would not supply in a few hours
all the human material for a new Polar voyage,
or an expedition to the upper waters of the
Nile, utterly regardless of climate ; and when
the question is one of furnishing a Soudan con-
tingent to look for a lost crank in a hot and
howling wilderness, experience shows that the
Australian can camp out for months in a worse
climate than the worst his own land produces,
that the only real danger lies in drinking too
much whisky while he is there, and being
officially thanked in the middle of a wintry
deluge when he comes back. He is better fitted
to toil on a tropical plantation than either the
Chinaman, the Kanaka, or, perhaps, even the
negro, and the theory that these “inferior
races ” can labour where the Aryan cannot is a
hollow lie from beginning to end. When the
Panama Railway was in course of construction
the work was done chiefly by Africans, because
the European was said to be unable to face the
heat of the Central American forests, and it is
estimated that that little railroad cost the life
of one African for every yard that was con-
structed. The Suez Canal was made by Egyptian
workmen and they died off by thousands in the
process. The cheap coloured labourer, in fact,
often perishes five or six times faster than
the European when engaged in hard labour at
the tropics, but he has three great advantages
which recommend him to the Queensland
planter. He is ignorant and servile, therefore
he can be kept at work by means of kicks
and execrations, no matter what the surround-
ing conditions may be ; he is of no particular
importance, so that ten of him may die in an
insalubrious cane-field, and be buried with less
fuss than would be made over one white
labourer ; and, above all, he is cheap. And, on
the other hand, the whiteman has two disad-
vantages. The planter is still haunted by
memories of the old slave regime as it used to
exist in the West Indies and the Southern
States, and he absolutely refuses to recognise
that unskilled labour on a plantation should be
paid on the same scale as any other labour that
is equally hard and uncomfortable ; in short,
because the old slave nigger used to do planta-
tion work, he considers that the white man
should do it on the nigger’s terms, and the
white man naturally disagrees. Also, because
the plantation nigger used to sleep anywhere,
he considers that the white labourer should be
willing to hang out in a box, or a wheelbarrow,
or under a tree, and again the latter disagrees.
Apart from all these things, there is no tropical
labour problem at all. There is no place in
Australia where the capitalist can hang out
that the Australian workman will not hang out
also—if he is paid for doing it. If he is not
paid, he naturally refuses to hang out—that is
the whole secret. There is no question of de-
veloping the resources of the North ; the reduc-
tion of the Australian workman’s wages is the
one thing aimed at.
One Bushranger, One Vote.
An affluent citizen named R. G. Benson writes
in the following strain in the Argus. There is
a certain fitness in this, for, judging by the
letter, Benson is just the sort of individual
who ought to write to the Argus, and the
Argus is just the sort of paper that an indivi-
dual like Benson should write to
Sir,—At a short distance from my residence there
stands a cottage occupied by a once notorious bushranger,
who has cost the colony hundreds if not thousands of
pounds, and done much evil. I have been in the colony
42 years, and have endeavoured to do some good for my
fellow-colonists. Now, sir, docs the Premier or the leader
■of the Opposition consider me so credulous as to believe
in their sincerity when they state that this retired hush-
.langcr is entitled to the same elective suffrage as I am ?
This is the argument which the whole Conserva-
tive party travels on. Taking it all round,
however, The Bulletin is inclined to give its
vote for the retired bushranger for several
reasons. Firstly, the world knows pretty well
the amount of evil which he has done, while the
amount which may or may not have been done
by that affluent citizen, Mr. Benson, is still an
unknown quantity, and an individual who
would write such a letter looks as if he might
do a good deal of evil. Therefore, this paper
would rather trust the bushranger with a vote
than it would Mr. Benson, the former being a
known and the latter an unknown quantity.
Secondly, if a bushranger can’t repent enough
to have a vote on earth he can hardly repent
enough to go to Paradise, unless voting is a
tougher job than going to Paradise ; and this
consideration seems to knock the whole Chris-
tian religion on the head, and if the Christian
religion is knocked on the head it is hardly
worth while for Mr. Benson to write to the
papers stating that he has done good for 42
years, for the whole affair doesn’t matter. This
consideration is advanced chiefly because the
writer of the letter is evidently a hardened and
incurable Christian—in fact, nobody except his
kind of Christian would ever have provided
such a document. Thirdly, the law doesn’t
refuse the ex-bushranger a plural vote, or a
dozen plural votes, because he is an ex-bush-
ranger, but because he lives in a cottage and
doesn’t own property ; if the retired solicitor of
the high-roads had enough property he would
have 20 votes, and if all the 12 apostles and the
fathers of the early church pigged it in the
cottage in his stead, they would have only one.
Consequently the One-man-one-vote question
does not affect this matter in the least, and Mr.
Benson in advertising his own goodness and
trying to blast his neighbour’s character commits
a wholly gratuitorrs hunk of meanness. It
would be quite as much to the point if he
wrote, stating that there was a notorious ex-
criminal in an adjacent house, and demanding
to know if, considering this circumstance, wheat
was going to go up the year after next. Still,
when Benson thuds down his retired bush-
ranger in the solid columns of the Akgus, and
asks if, in the face of such a circumstance as
this, plural voting shall be abolished, the flabby
statement almost looks like an argument, and
may even pass for one in certain quarters.
Toryism has travelled for a good many years on
just such arguments as this, and the only reason
why it can’t travel much longer, is that the
world has gotdead-tired of the individual who (by
reason of our crookedly-adjusted social system)
plunders in a bank, and the other individual
who used to plunder in the bush might be a
pleasant change. Anyhow, The Bulletin is
in a Scriptural mood to-day, and it remembers .
that there is more than one repentant thief who
makes a good show in Holy Writ, while there
is not one repentant bank or reformed building
society in the whole volume, and hardly one
really square capitalist, and, therefore, when a
wealthy and influential citizen has the cast-iron
front to write, stating that he has done good
for 42 years, it feels doubtful if he ought to
have any vote at all.
♦ ——
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. claims to have
practically solved the sugar enigma and to have
proved the Central Mill system. Its estate of
Homebush, near Mackay, has been split up into
small sugar farms. Each tenant signs two
documents—a lease and an agreement. The
lease is for five years, with the right of pur-
chase at any time within three years—one-
fifth cash and four annual instalments ; price,
for bush land, £ 1 per acre, and for cultivated
land, £5 per acre. The agreement simply binds
the farmer to grow a certain quantity of cane
and patronise tlie Company’s mill. The Com-
pany pays 13s. per ton for cane; when its
patrons’ area of cane reaches 600 acres, it will
pay 13s. 6d. per ton, and 900 acres, 14s. per
ton. Rations will be advanced, if necessary,
to the limit of 10s. per week, and in some
cases horses and implements are supplied on
terms. Over 1000 acres have already been
taken up, and applications are so numerous that
the Company can pick its tenants. It seems
that black labour is not necessary at all to
preserve the sugar industry. Neither is black
nor yellow labour needed in the Northern
Territory, in spite of the impudent assertions
of an engine-driving Governor and some military
barnacles.
♦
Whom do the Labour-party represent ? Wage-
earners primarily; toilers with hand or head’
mind or muscle, whether in the shearing-shed,
down the mine, at the forge, in the factory,
behind the counter, or at the desk. What are
the qualifications for the representation of
Labour ? It is not absolutely essential that its
representative should have been a worker,
either with hand or head. The man who has
been overworked, either mentally or physically,
cannot have had the time necessary for the
study of economic problems, and, therefore,
cannot intelligently represent Labour. The
worker whose democracy is not the result
of thought but simply the growth of
environment, a consequence of birth in
lowly circumstances, may—nay, does—in many
instances, become a conservative on elevation
above his primary social state. It is, therefore,
evident that the sole qualifications for Labour
representation are sympathy and a knowledge
of economic law as applied to social problems.
The son of a wealthy man, educated at a
university, reared in the lap of luxury, having
espoused democratic principles and shown his
unselfishness, his courage, the universality of
his sympathy, by daring to make public declara-
tion of democratic faith, not fearing loss of
social caste or family friendships, has thereby
given the very highest proof of fitness for
Labour representation. If such sympathy could
be found, allied to high intellectuality, and pro-
found knowledge of social problems, there would
stand revealed an ideal leader of democracy.
Your candidate whose sole qualifications are
10 hours passed previously in daily labour, has
small claims for consideration.

I must honestly confess that I felt rather “good”
and self-sacrificing, when I gave up the “Theodora”
primiiire on 30th ult., to attend a private performance of
amateurs at the Royal Standard of two (!) new plays by a
local man—Mr. Lester Bellingham—but the unselfish
feeling wore off during the evening. The first, a broad
farce, entitled "Glanamman Villa,” was really amusing,
altho* interpreted by amateurs—two, at least, of whom
would wreck the productions of Pinero if he gave them
the chance ; but the others, Misses Nixon, Cooper and E,
Grant-Cooper performed very fairly. Head and shoulders
over the company Avas the author, as Donald Clackman-
nan, an entomologist, got-up like CartAvright in “The
Middleman,” and the fun of the thing was caused by his
beetles getting loose among the company. His consoling
explanation (as nearly as I can remember it), when the
bugs are upset again and terror reigns supreme, “Don’t
be afeeh), Teddies! ’t(p only the Magnis Crustie—never
known io'tfalk,” brought down the house. The dialogu i
Avas good and natural, and the characters were alive.
Allowing for the awful handicap of being played by
amateurs—the best of whom always has something
uncanny about him—“ Glanamman Villa” went so well
that, Avith a little judicious doctoring by someone who
knoAvs the stage, it Avould make an amusing curtain-raiser
at any theatre. The second piece, “ The Tempter,” had
a dramatically concluded first act, but the second was
wordy, pointless, and ended very cheaply, especially for
the Aveak young man Avho “ preaches” so babblingly. For
one thing, instead of the betrayed Miriam (disappointingly

house.
Talking about “ evening-dress” houses, what makes me

The third performance of “ Theodora ” at Sydney Royal
aa as varied by a crash of O.P. scenery in the circus-scene
and it was lovely to see how Duquesne folded Sara pro
tectingly in his arms and both their heads popped out
through an aperture in the falling wall, while the audi-
ence cheered and the divine one doubtless longed to be a
real Theodora for a minute to order the scene-shifters out
to be hanged in a body. Theodora wears two really gyp
attires of the fancy-dress variety. In the first act she
looks something like a Pope, for the head is covered with
a mitre-shaped cap covered in jewels, and in her hands
she carries what looks like an Annunciation lily. The
long, trained cloakish covering was of yellow satin brocaded
thickly in gold under cover of brown-golden coloured
gauze embroidered with medallions of cherub heads,
sandals (they do say those little radish-like toes are only
painted “ fingered ” stockings) and long red hair. In the
fourth act a delicious mingling of crushed strawberry,
white, and a kind of pearl grey, all designed in gold—a
silver net round the hair. Fifth act, ditto, minus the
upper covering of white, which made the gown all
pinkish, scarf shawl of cream mill, spangled in gold. In
the sixth act, sea-green court train from shoulders
embroidered in glittering colours, gold predominating;
under dress of blue and mass of scarlet and blue and
green and gold design ; jewels everywhere—a gorgeous
array which went well, and the throng of regardlessly-
gotten-up Australian supes, who betrayed their origin by
hanging out to watch Sara with their eyes and mouths
open. In the last act, cream mill over green silk,
designed in gold—“ cestus ” (she wears one all the time)
in gold.
There were hardly any dresses in the circle. One
woman looked charming in a buttercup gown, with
puffed Tosca sleeves of chiffon, and huge black and gold
fan ; two of the ugliest old parties there had the most
nappy diamonds in the place ; and an obviously and
badly-rouged young man (ugh !) in the prompt stage-box
tried to modify his inartistic blush by shading it behind
his companion’s big red fire-screen fan.

Some of the Australian dailies argue that
the third party in the N.S.W. Legislature can
never attain its ends, and cite the comparative
failure of the Parnellites in the House of Com-
mons, the Local Option party in various legisla-
tures, and the R.C. party of O’Shanassy, in Vic-
toria, in proof of the statement. The position of
the N.S. Wales Labour-party is not on all-fours
with that of any of the cliques referred to, who, in
every case, only represented a small section of
out-door public opinion and had to fight anta-
gonistic governments and oppositions, inside.
The new N.S.W. party, on the contrary, represents
the opinions of the majority outside the N.S.W,
Assembly and, inside, every member is pledged to
uphold nearly all the measures they advocate.
Their mission is, therefore, not one of internal or
external propaganda, it is simply to accomplish
the community’s determination to have reforms
legalised whose justice all, save bigots, admit;
measures the people saw ho likelihood of obtaining
while the ins and outs wasted the country’s time
in an endless struggle for place, to the utter
neglect of all real business. The Labour-party are
not hunting for billets; they are striving after
long-demanded, long-desired legislation; if they
keep this end in view it must be attained. They
are simply a new force for compelling the cum-
brous parliamentary machine to perform its work.
The fall of the Bank of Van Diemen’s
Land is due purely to silver-share-gamble over-
drafts. The bank will now have silver-scrip to
sell by the cwt. The reported overdrafts read
sensationally—one Hobart firm, £80,000; another,
£40,000; Zeehan (silver district) branch, £36,000,
one firm of brokers there being £9000 “ beyond
the constable,” and a Hobart firm of brokers,
£6000. Altogether the bank advanced £1,030,763;
how much to “good marks” is uncertain. The
capital of the bank is £540,000—paid up, £250,000;
reserve liability, £250,000; reserve fund, £40,000.
The last half-yearly report (June 16) showed coin
and bullion only to the extent of £70,432, while its
debts to the public alone were £950,319, its total
liabilities being £1,257,033, against which it relied
on the advances already mentioned, and bills and
debts due by other banks. The directors re-
ported a profit available for distribution
of £18,714, and declared a dividend of 9 per
cent., free of dividend tax; added £2000 to
reserve ; and carried forward, £5042. The sudden
demand to pay up will probably break several
big firms, and these will re-act on a shoal of small
storekeepers; otherwise the depositors will not
suffer, though the shareholders are likely to be
pushed. The other banks are holding off. The
closure is a telling illustration of the privileges
chartered banks obtain from the community, and
affords reason for examining the arguments
concerning the establishment of a State Bank,
even if all the existing charters to banks of issue
have to bo annulled and the financial corporations
become mere money-lending agencies.

“The Profligate,” it is understood, was
written by Pinero, in reply to critics who stated
that he was incapable of evolving a high-class
serious play. Surely no one who has been
able to sit out this prolonged sob, will tempt
the author, by repeating the statement to pen
another theatrical vindication of his claims to
rank among the serious writers. It seems to us,
moreover, that his time might have been more
profitably occupied in writing sermons for barren-
brained parsons, for to our irreverent mind this
play is the most lugubrious and lachyrmose of
maudlin and modern stage-weeps. Its senti-
ment is false, sickly and gushy; its pathos is
hysterical and lacks the convincing ring of the
genuine article. There isn’t a healthy flesh-and-
blood man or woman in it. Instead it is per-
vaded by atrabilious spectres who shriek and sigh,
gasp and groan, preach and pose, through four
weary acts of histrionic neuralgia. Furthermore, it
is unfortunate that all the leading impersonators
of play, at Sydney Garrick, are over-
much given to preaching at the best of times.
Finding in it much room for the exercise of this
unfortunate propensity, they let themselves go
with such zeal that one almost expects the ushers
to take up a collection during each interval.

All the long-absent artistes Avere warmly
received by a packed house on opening-night
Avhen Olga Nethersole and Charles Cartwright
received numerous “ calls ” after each act. Per-
haps we have been spoiled by Sara and Sardou,
but the dainty little lady seemed to gasp more
persistently, to turn her a’s into e’s more fre
quently and to declaim the most conversational of
remarks more vigorously than ere her departure.
Nevertheless, her copious weeping during the last
two acts, like her joyful gasping during the first
two, Avas rapturously received. CartAvright’s
profligate Avas undoubtedly well played, but he was
too loud for this little theatre, and at times the
show assumed the aspect of a contest between lie
and Cautley as to Avho should rant in the highest
key. The latter, hoAvever, has an unfair advan-
tage, his raving and sobbing commences with the
first act, Avhile Cartwright’s opportunities, in
those directions, are confined to the third and final
acts. Young Cosgrove, much improved as an
actor, also wails vigorously during the later acts,
as does Mary Kingsley; Avhile Nellie Lyons (a
great favourite Avho hasn’t been seen in Sydney
since she played in “The Middleman”) is
lachrymose and limp from ring-up to ring-down.
A very faint touch of comedy is supplied by Frank
Cates (as a travelled rake, on the lines of his Major
Tarver in “ Dandy Dick ”), and by A. C. Poulton,
as a loAv-comedy valet. Harold Russell re-appears
in a small part, and the occasion was made interest-
ing to a certain section of people by the appearance
of Miss Constance Edwards, a Sydney amateur,
daughter of a local lady critic, in the ranks of pro-
fessional theatricals. “The Profligate,” well-
staged and very Avell played, has some strong
situations and many beautiful lines, but, to our
mind, lacks much that is necessary to ensure
popularity.

“Honest Hearts and Willing Hands ” has
struck Sydney at a mighty cold season. In the
first place, Sara Bernhardt has elevated the
standard of judgment to a hitherto unattained
altitude, and brawn has thereby suffered when
brought into direct comparison Avith brains; next,
the management shut out the unthinking who
might have filled the galleries by an uncalled-for
rise in prices; lastly, a name without something
to back it up Avon’t draAv in Australia worth a
cent. Folks who would gladly have paid a round
sum to see one of the greatest sluggers the Avorld
ever produced performing professionally don’t feet
the least curiosity to see him in the role
of the Avorld’s worst actor, and their common-
sense and artistic perception is, doubtless, most
laudable. Indeed, it would have been just one
whit more ridiculous to have expected Sullivan to
have filled the Town Hall by his performance of
an organ solo. Therefore is the atmosphere chilly
at Her Majesty’s, particularly in the vicinity of
the treasury. Everyone must sympathise with
the disappointed stranger in a far land, and there-
fore Duncan B. Harrison, who is a good and
genial fellow, deserves to be let down lightly;
nevertheless, it must be said that his play, Avritten
on the lines of the conventional Irish drama, is
not artistically designed for the introduction of a
slugging-match. The boxer and the tussle are too
palpably Avedged into the story. The fight should
have been made the culminating feature of the
plot. A play might have been written showing
now pugilists are reared and trained. First act:
Sullivan leaving his Irish home with all his goods
and chattels in a red handkerchief; second
act, arrival in America, followed by a quarrel
with his senior in the police, and dismissal
after putting the inspector to sleep ; third
act, Sullivan’s training quarters—with stoutly-
resisted temptations at the hands of amorous
duchesses, the entreaties of a lovely and pious
colleen, champagne-bearing dudes and bribe-
bringing bookmakers; fourth and final act, the
ring-side at early morn, the bottle-holders, the
referee, with loud thuds, splashes of gore, the
cries of “books” and yells of backers, then the
triumph of Sullivan, and the joy of the loving and

One feature of the Bernhardt season nas been
the manner in which the artful James has caused
the waits to be filled up with high-class music,
rendered by a strong orchestra. Ihese waits, by
the way, are chiefly caused by the filtering behind
of purring old tabbies, lisping dudes, and tame-
cat critics, who hinder the divine Sara from com-
posing her nerves and changing duds. Another
feature of the Bernhardt season is that the audi-
torium lights are never lowered during the per-
formance. This greatly assists those who study
French by book, but is bad for the eye-
sight of those who study it by means of
opera-glass. “Theodora” has undoubtedly re-
vived the waning glories of Sara’s sea-
son She fetches them in it all the
time. Darmont, as Andreas, has come out of his
shell and shown an unexpected vigour, earnest-
ness, and ease. It is better and more harmoniously
dressed and wigged than anything they’ve yet
staged, and the freshness of the scenery, by
Goatcher, Hennings, Grist, and Clint, though
admittedly hurriedly prepared, is a relief after the
cracks and crumples of the much-travelled French
stuff. After five nights of “Theodora,” at the
Royal, “La Tosca ” was revived on Wednesday
night; “Pauline Blanchard” goes up again to-
night (Thursday), “La Tosca” again on Friday
night, and “ Camille ’’ ends the Bernhardt Sydney
season, one of her most wonderful triumphs in a
small city. As previously announced, she sails
for America in the Mariposa, and “The Old
Guard,” with the music of Planquette and the
words of Farnie, fills the bill on Monday.
** * *
At Sydney Criterion our old friend, “Dr.
Bill,” seems quite as popular as of yore, for, up to
date, S.R.O. business has prevailed. In many
places the author has been unproved on by the
filling in of gaps and the spicing of weak situa-
tions, chiefly at the hands of George Anson, who,
however, repeats his “at the age of thirty-five
years ” speech just a little too often. The only
change from the original cast lies in the substitu-
tion of George Gordon for Mr. Clitherow. Pos-
sibly, the show goes better now than ever, though
occasionally it shows a tendency to subside after
a disconcerting, if mirth-arousing, gag has broken
the thread of discourse. Myra Kemble’s per-
formance is a great improvement on her original
effort; she has worked in many little strokes of
telling business, but has occasionally some
moments when she appears to have run down.
All the other folks, from “Bob” Brough to
Brenda Gibson, shine with B. & B.’s best theatri-
cal gloss, and half the good things are drowned in
the laughter rising from the auditorium.

When Bridget O’Brien, Esq., winds up a
most successful season at Adelaide Royal, up goe s
“Les Cloches de Corneville,” with Johnny Sheri-
dan as Gaspard, Warwick Gainor as the Marquis,
Gracie Whiteford as Serpolette, and Annie
Nelson, a local songstress, as Germaine. This
will close the season. Tommy Hudson is not
getting tired of making money at Garner’s Rooms,
and surrounded by a capable company he
is likely to do well till his departure for India in
September. Tanner goes on with his fast, and
the Cyclorama is still an attraction. Achurch-
Charrington Co. are at Broken Hill putting in a
week. The success of the Patey season was inter-
fered with by Madame catching a bad cold.
** * *
“The Dancing Girl” thrives no better
than was expected at Melbourne Royal. Why
H. A. Jones’s beautifully varnished work should
fall so flat is a question which the local critics
answer in about 15 different ways, yet a fair,
rough and ready explanation of the trouble seems
to stick right out. The play ruthlessly exposes
all the weakness of the company, whilst individual
performers caricature several unnatural features
of the play. Save for a superabundance of cheap
philosophy, the dialogue is equal to the author’s
reputation. “Don’t preach at me,” says the dook
as he carefully poses Ids noble proportions upon a
lump of his sea-girt domains. “I won’t—l will
wait till you are under the horse’s hoofs,” replies
the morbid young Sybil with the game leg. But
a lot of preaching goes on, nevertheless, the Dook
of Gooseberry (called Guiseberry, by mistake)
being the most inveterate sermonizer of the whole
crowd. There can be no doubt that the two stars
are mainly responsible for the sum total of dull-
ness, although minor offenders contribute
according to their means. Miss Laura Villiers
is an unattractive actress, intelligent, but
devoid of charm a crude, thin, restless
suggestion of Genevieve Ward without the
mellow tones or nervous force of that
remarkable woman. It is soul, rather than art,
which Miss Villiers lacks, though to be sure she
is unintentionally pathetic when she throws up
her arms and cries, “ I must have my public to
worship me.” They don’t worship her, dash it
all ! The lady is never absurd, however, now
that her moonlight dance has been mercifully
withdrawn from circulation. Three or four pro-
miscuous whirls were all she did last Saturday,
instead of repeating her former painful struggles
to afford the dook a fleeting glimpse of her stock-
ing. This amendment robs the young Quaker’s
conundrum, “Woman, what art thou?” of much
significance, and causes the curta n to descend
without a smile. One feels sorry that the old
dance has been abandoned, because it was so good
for the liver—the audience’s liver. It was, indeed.
Clifton Alderson, as the gigantic gooseberry,
improves very little on acquaintance. Poor
houses and indifferent notices have toned him
down, and imparted a touch of melancholy to his
evening-party manner, but Clifton is still very
much on the surface. His desperate resolve to
commit suicide is as hollow as his airy abandon.
Also his personal beauty and swelling bosom are
unsuited to the peerage, although they qualify
him to give a magnificent performance of a
heroic shop-walker. There is nothing of the
unsophisticated islander about Julius Knight,
who plays an Adam Bede character in a
thoroughly town-bred, yet bright and manly,
style. The Dancing Girl’s aged parent is much
overweighted with his woe, and another painful
apparition is the dock’s chum, a young chappie
worthy to be promptly interred in the same sar-
cophagus as the dock’s aunt. Auntie is really
too dreadful under present circumstances. _ Pro-
vidence has afflicted her with a falsetto voice of
extended range, and unless the management can
throw in a song and dance for Gooseberry’s blood
relation, or persuade her to cork her face, she
had better retire on a pension forthwith. Miss
Gwendolen Floyd, as the lame Sybil, shows no
indications of being other than a moderate actress
in a winsome part, whilst the balance —excepting,
perhaps, old Crake and an exaggerated flunkey—
seem incapable of proving moderate in any parts.
A newspaper correspondence re the quality of
Miss Villiers & Co. may serve to arouse popular
interest in their dire failure, and so prolong the
run of “The Dancing Girl.” At time of writing,
however, a new piece was in rehearsal for
Saturday next.
* * * *
“Huy Bias ” is negotiating its sixth lap at
the Princess’, and attendances, which dropped off
during three nights when Nellie Farren was
absent, have bobbed up again with much of their
old serenity. Nellie’s understudy, Miss Ethel
Blenheim, was not an exhilarating change. The
difference she made to the ensemble ought to prove
instructive to a number of young people who don’t
appreciate the amount of burlesque experience
and real brain capital that the evergreen Farren
puts into Gaiety business. The lengthening of
the present season to 12 weeks has not yet been
arranged with manager Edwardes, of London, but
“ Cinder-Ellen ” —a more gorgeous show, in the
matter of scenery and properties, than any opera
or pantomime yet staged under Williamsonian
management—may be seen anon, anyway.
* * * *
“Peril,” at Melbourne Bijou, comes a
little nearer to the French original than our old
familiar “ Friends,” but one adaptation of Sardou
is sufficiently like the other to make very little
difference. The dangers of Platonic attachments
between young wives and bachelor captains are
fully exposed in “ Perillikewise the absurdity
of a trusting husband who seems to glory in being
insulted by his circle of acquaintances is depicted
with a free hand. Unfortunately, the play
wobbles between the sublime and the ridiculous,
and at the finish one gropes in vain for its moral
lesson. There is not a genuine ring about the
narrative at all, for the chief characters do impos-
sibly apropos things at critical moments. It is a
farcical tragedy in four acts, beautifully mounted,
well acted, and rounded off with a dead hare.
Titheradge comes well to the fore as Dr. Thornton,
the cynic who looks faithfully after the interests
of his soft-headed host and listens at the keyhole
whilst his hostess is being chased round the
library by the lover she has fooled into false hopes.
Boucicault, as a sort of titled Gunnion, making
himself “ darned unpleasant ” on principle, is very
funny, and a cantankerous couple of friends
are thoroughly well played by Lawrence and Miss
Romer. Lyndal, too, is a fairly convincing, and
certainly handsome, picture of the seductive senti-
mentalist, whose rash pursuit of a shadow gives
the play its name ; but Sass is cruelly placed as
soft-headed Sir George Ormond. His attempt to
seem ingenuously simple is bright and pleasant
enough, yet it can’t be taken quite seriously,
because he ain’t built that way. Mrs, Brough
comes well through the furnace in her trying
character of Lady Ormond without making the
very most of her opportunities in the love-chase
scene, and Miss Spong is delightful as her step-
daughter. No notice of a Bijou show should
forget to tip the stage servants, who are always
first quality at this house. MHntyre is the most
finished specimen of a “ gentleman’s-gentleman ”
that ever brushed a coat behind the footlights.
“ Peril ” should last over another week, anyhow.

A look of dull despair has come over the
Opera House. The mechanical race scene con-
tinues to go off like an alarm clock at 10.30, but
interest in “The County Fair” has gone off once
and for all. The local press-liars, too, seem to
ignore the MacMahons and their brilliant enter-
prises now that the brethren have reduced their
advt. to two inches, and the average newspaper
reader must sometimes wonder why “The County
Fair” doesn’t get the puff paragraphs that were
erstwhile wasted on weaker entertainments at the
same house. The Macs have not been lucky with
their Murr’kan play, which would be by no means
bad if it were not for the race-scene, by which
alone it is judged. No matter, the last nights of
Mif 9 Prue, Tag'gs, and that dear boy Otis are
announced, and the free-list is entirely suspended.

It is a shudderful fact that Alf. Boothman might have
found a watery grave in the Melbourne flood, but didn’t.
Early on that memorable Sabbath morning Mr. and Mrs.
B. were aroused from slumber by a gondolier tilting the
butt-end of his merry bark against their bedroom window
and recommending them to be rowed away ere it was too
late. “For in that sleep of death what dreams may
come,” shouts the gondolier, being aware that the re-
nowned hactor is “nuts” on “Hamlet,” to which Alf.
replied—“ Once aboard the lugger, all is well,” and pre-
pared to turn his military back upon the old, old home he
loved so well. There wasn’t much time to spare, unfor-
tunately. The pond was rising rapid ly, and Alf. was only
able to pack up Mrs. Boothmai, a tooth-brush, and half-a-
dozen pet canaries, or so, before the gallant craft shoved
off and carried them o’er the murky bosom of the aquatic
carnival to a dry place. Alfred the Great was saved!

“ Experience in all branches of the profession.” Such
is the tempting ad. in a last week’s Melbourne paper for
a young lady or gentleman to join a provincial theatrical
company. Oh, that experience—in all branches, includ-
ing how to live without eating ! Ask Rev. Macully.
George Rignold will be heartily welcomed in Melbourne,
and there is every sign that the Royal will soon be
brightened with the limelight of his presence. Long is it
since he appeared due South, but in Melbourne his
Henry V., Armytage in “ Lights o’ London,” Mephis-
topheles, and other parts are remembered as standard
impersonations.’
Madame Fanny Simonsen is brimful of charming anec
dotes of her discovery of her new operatic celebrities-
Such exciting interviews behind the scenes, and when she
visited a theatre the word went round as if she had been
the Queen of Italy.
“How did Coppin acquire his nickname of ‘Artful
Dodger?’” It was through acting in a farce of that
name, and the last time was at Melbourne Royal, when
he and Mrs. Bryer sang and danced the ever-popular
duct, “ The best of all possible Dod-ges !” Coppin’s great
songs were “ Yilikins," “ Constantinople,” and “ Old
Dog Tray.”
Manager Twinning has sent us a bundle of Yokohama
papers, tied together apparently with one of the fair Cora
Brown-Potter-Bellew’s stay-laces.
According to a letter received from Manager Twinning,
dated Colombo, Mr. and Mrs. Bellew and he were about
to sail for London where they open in September.
Twinning wants to know if Australia thinks Sara a copy
of Cora.
On the last night of “ Flying from Justice,” at Sydney
Royal, the actor-manager, George Rignold, gagged right
through the piece. Appearing in the forgers’ den, he was
asked by Tolano ; “ Who are you ? ” to which he replied :
“ I am John L. Sullivan ! ” and, Tolano looking surprised,
followed that up by saying: “ Don’t be frightened, I only
say that to mugs.”
There is much sympathy with David Lee, Melbourne
city organist, over the unexpected death of his wife,
sister of the late Miss Johnson, amateur actress, esteemed
in Melbourne society some years ago.
Dampier issues a creditable manifesto, closing the
Alexandra. He is the only Melbourne manager on the
London system of bringing out original plays, although
Garnet Walch has the monopoly as safe as David Lee of
the Town Hall organ.

tiie skirt of her dress over her head, when the representa-
tion is completely produced of a shell. A large number
of photos, of Miss Lind in this pose have been sold, though
there is naught visible of the artiste.

Max O’Rell left England for Australia last week. He
comes via the States, making his fourth visit to that
country, and will arrive in Sydney at the beginning of
next year.
Santley was a great failure in America, and, by the
London critics at the Handel Festival was considered as
completely finished. He, consequently, intends to return
to Australia and settle in Sydney.
Grade Warner is engaged to be married to a reporter
on the Adelaide Register, and Charlie, dear boy, approves
of the match.

close-fisted as they make ’em, will now wear the pants

Melbourne Tramway Co.’s net revenue,
year ending June 1891, is £48,464 below the
previous year.
Mr. T. K. Abbott, S.M., of Sydney, died
suddenly last week aged 46. He was an excellent
magistrate when in full working order, and
grasped the central point of a case while other
people talked all round it.
Mayor Pitt, of Collingwood, has been
elected M.L.C. for North Yarra Province, Vie.,
vice the late Dr. Beaney. The Labour-vote told
again. Pitt was the whole-hog Radical among
three, the other two being alleged Liberals.
The Republican cause in Australia gets a
strong fillip by the extended scale on which our
brethren, the citizens of Republican countries
such as Switzerland, America, and Prance, are
beginning to locally celebrate their national anni-
versaries.
Sydney Daily Telegraph’s average cir-
culation is certified as 35,092. Now, Alfred !
Apropos, this certificate emphasises the fact that
the circulation of Melbourne Age is just about
double that of the two Sydney morning papers
put together.
Parkes appears to have given 10 grs. of
strychnine in advance to his Electoral Act Amend-
ment Bill. The 53rd section makes it unlawful to
keep open any public-house on the day of polling.
As this constitutes an important amendment to
the Licensing Act, it appears outside the title and
scope of the Electoral Bill which it is contended
will have to be withdrawn and amended.
On the first day of term the N.S.W-
judges dolled themselves up in the most nonsensi'
cally gorgeous millinery—scarlet and ermine,
black and gold, and scarlet and mauve, but all the
Queen’s Counsel were absent—we hope, as an ex-
pression of disgust. Two ladies sat in the jury-
box, evidently much awed by the barbaric
grandeur in which this set of very commonplace
peojfft (the weakest bench in Australia, probably)
had tricked themselves out. The less brains a
man has, the more clothes he needs. The Full
Court, instead of dressing itself up as if for a
feast of long-pig at the Court of King Calcali of
Ashantee, should have come out into King-street
in sackcloth and ashes and gone down on its
narrowbones to do public penance for its wooden-
headed decision in the recent appeal-case of Dr
McLeod.
Parkes’ apparent contemplated treachery
to the cause of one-man-one-vote nearly ship-
wrecked his Ministry. Notwithstanding the fre-
quent visits of Bruce Smith to the Labour-party’s
“caucus,” the amendment, which affirmed that
womanhood-suffrage should remain in abeyance
until political equality was conferred upon the
country’s manhood, was defeated by 49 to 41.
The amendment was accepted as a vote of censure
by Parkes, but nevertheless the Ministry was un-
usually active in canvassing the help of the Labour-
party ; and Bruce Smith apparently expected great
difficulty in inducing sufficient Labour members
to vote against Fitzgerald's amendment to save
the Ministry from a humiliating defeat on a
“fad.” It was managed with some dexterity, for
just before the tellers were named, the numbers,
instead of standing at ayes 41, noes 49, stood at
ayes 47, noes 43. Six members, chiefly “ Labour
members,” as if by a preconcerted arrangement,
crossed over to the “ noes ” as the Speaker rose to
name the tellers. The debate on Parkes abstract
motion cost £2BO in members’ allowances alone.
Is Parkes’ championship of womanhood suffrage
ruled by the lady that “rocks the cradle” at
Hampton Cottage? He is encouraging the idea
that his Ministry is a one-man Ministry, and that
that man is very much under petticoat govern-
ment.
In The Bulletin’s opinion, the N.S.W.
Labour-party made its first big political mistake
when it tolerated, without r -snonstrance, the
appointment of Dan O’Conr r to the Upper
House, in face of the fact that) he had been twice
rejected by popular constituencies. The Bul-
letin would not object, under existing circum-
stances, to the nomination to the Legislative
Council of a politician who had many times been
elected to the Assembly, and whose connection
with the representative chamber, as in Mr.
O’Connor’s case, had been severed through no
disgraceful or corrupt act; but it emphatically
protests against any executive officer of the Gov-
ernment being sent to the Upper House after he
has lost the confidence of the Assembly electors.
When Parkes said he didn’t appoint O’Connor
“ until he saw that he had a majority in the Assem-
bly,’’the Labour-party (through whose supporters
O’Connor had been kept out of the Lower House)
should have told him then and there that he had
no majority without them, and that, as he had'
not consulted them, it was impudent for him to
presume on their approval. Parkes, it is true,
might have resigned on this, but then he is
obviously seeking a dramatic opportunity for
making a temporary retirement, and it is quite
unlikely that he would accept this as a resignation
pretext, considering how loudly he had opposed
the nomination of Suttor under similar condi-
tions, and how undemocratic was his side of the
present case.
