Tales of Old Sydney.
No. XI.—“Benefit o’ Clargy Bill.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
One November day, in one of the earlier-
years of the reign of His Most Excellent Majesty
the Fourth George, the Court of King’s Bench*
Westminster, was presided over by Mr. Justica
Bayley. Three other ennined creatures kept his
Lordship company, but with them we have
nothing to do.
The case before the court is that of William
Henry Ellis, who was convicted at the last Devoir
assizes of having stolen six shillings, the property
of Susan Newman, widow, in whose employ he
had been tvs shopman. .
That an offence which, though capital, stillto>
that enlightened age appeared a paltry one, shouldl
come before the august King’s Bench, was a fact
so curious as to need some preliminary explana-
tion.
The explanation is to be found in the circum-
stance that, in the first count of the indictment,,
the prisoner was charged with stealing “ the?
property of Susan Newman,” and in the secondl
count, with stealing “ the property of Susan
Newman and the different members of her family.”'
Now, notwithstanding that Ellis had descended!
to petty pilferings, he had not been deserted by ,
all his friends. Some of them briefed counsel to
argue in favour of upsetting the conviction.
Mr. Chitty and Mr. Coleridge were his counsel,
and they made a strenuous effort to arrest judg-
ment. For four hours they battled bra vely against
the prosecution and the court. But their labour
was unavailing. After consulting with his brother
Lordships, Judge Bayley pronounced against
them, and sentenced the prisoner “to be trans-
ported beyond the seas for the term of seven years,
to such place as His Majesty, by and with the
advice of his Privy Council, shall be pleased to
direct and appoint; and that, in the meantime,
he shall be committed to the custody of the keeper
of His Majesty’s Gaol at Newgate.”
This was sufficiently satisfactory so far as it all
went. So, at least, the prisoner thought as with a
sigh so deep in its relieved breathing that it ex-
pressed the sudden relaxation of an extreme
tension of feeling. He had expected to be hanged,
he remarks as he passes dazedly from the bar.
But stay ! There is still time for him to be sen-
tenced to death. He is called back by his Honor
Judge Bayley, and he tremblingly resumes the
place where he has stood for four hours past, and
waits for his Lordship to speak.
“You have been brought back, prisoner, because
—h’m —ah —a slight omission has been made.”
The prisomr heard as though he heard not, for
his recall to the bar had driven him again into au
agony o c despair.
“ Mr. Clerk,” continued the Judge, “call upon
the prisoner.”
Mr. Dealton, clerk, obeyed. “Prisoner, ishab
have you to say why the Court should not pro-
ceed to pass judgment of death, and award execu-
tion against you ? ”
Ellis stammered, “Sir —I don’t—l don t
know. I —I— ” .
“ Leave it to your counsel,” whispered some
one in the public seats, in pity for the prisoner’s
too obvious distress.
“ I —leave it —to my counsel —my Lord 1” tho
accused at last managed to say.
“ No, your counsel can do nothing for you!”
declared the presiding judge.
But Coleridge iiad murmured a hint while the
Judge was speaking. “ Kneel, kneel, and pray
for the benefit of clergy !”
And Ellis mechanically knelt, and prayed tho
benefit of clergy. He did not know the signifi-
cance of the words —that was plain from his con-
fused utterance; but they served to turn the
hangman's hand aside for the time being.
For the Court allowed him the “ benefit of
clergy,” and instead of hanging him by the neck
till ho was dead, consented to repeat its former
verdict, and accordingly confirmed its order for
transportation.
» • #
That was the first occasion on which Ellis
pleaded the benefit of clergy. Ho pleaded it
again at the Antipodes later.
He “ went out ”in the Minstrel. His record on
board was “fair.” Chained for the best part of
each day of the voyage to a brother-transport and
the deck —the Surgeon of the Minstrel was
esteemed a splendid fellow by the Superintendent
of the Transport Office, because he was an excel-
lent disciplinarian—he had no opportunity of
indulging his pilfering tastes. This circumstance
was somewhat unfortunate for him, as, had he
been granted a chance of stealing frequently and
regularly, there is not the least reason to suppose
the Surgeon would have neglected to treat him as
he had treated at least one other similarly in-
clined. Had lie been “hanged at the yard-arm
for manifesting incurable thievish habits,” to use
the Surgeon’s phrase in the indent of a former
voyage, William Henry Ellis would have been
saved much suffering and not a little crime. Even
Captain Maconoohie, tenderest-hearted of penal
administrators, considered it was the truest mercy
to hang some men. Ellis was one such. He
should, in his own interests, have been hanged
before he was.
For the cause stated, however, his ship-record
was “ fair.” He was not of a violent temper, and
would submit to any amount of oppression, legal
or other, before he would resist. Hence, he did
not form a member of the class who “ ovalled ”
their irons when they should have kept them per-
fect, who struck their officials when they should
have saluted them, and who raised hideous noises
when silence was imperative. He was a pilferer,
not a mutineer; thievish, but not otherwise
insuliordiuate. Consequently, we say, his record
was fair.
On rare occasions the possession of a “fair”
record proved an advantage to a transport; on
many, a positive disadvantage.
In this way. When the transports were
mustered on landing by the Honourable the
Colonial Secretary, the list of “availables for
assignments ” would be made out. Tho official
families had first pick, and, naturally enough,
they would snap up the individuals with the fair
and good reports. And, nine times out of ten, to
be assigned to an official, was to be signed to a
capricious and tyrannical employer. Generally
speaking, there was only one worse person to be
under than an official—and that was the illiterate
Emancipist. The former from a sense of absolute
and irresponsible power; the latter, from innate
brutality, or tire idea of revenging upon somebody
the injuries he had himself received from the
System, were, by every principle of humanity,
ineligible for holding assigned hands. And, there-
fore, so beautifully was the System elaborated,
the percentage of assignments to them was larger
than to free settlers.
Now, Ellis, per Minstrel, having a fair report,
and Major Ovens being, admittedly, one of the
most brilliant officials of an epoch which was
adorned by General Darling, Dr. Douglass, Saxe
Bannister, and other gentry of like kidney, the
Fates, of course, ordained that unto Major Ovens,
should be assigned transport Ellis. A kind but
firm master might have saved Ellis : a master who
was firm to the degree of brute obstinacy, and who
constantly acted up to his magisterial declaration
“ that a convict’s feelings could not be considered,
because every convict was dead in law ” could
only be expected to “break” him. And break
him he did most effectually.
« * «
“ You’re a petty thief, then, are you,
Ellis?” said Ovens. “ Now, my man, I want you
as a stableman and you’ll have the best opportuni-
ties for stealing. Steal if you like! I lock noth-
ing up in my house —every assigned servant I have
can rob me as much as he or she likes. But look
ye here, pris’ner! For every pen’orth of food
wasted or property stolen, I give a lash ! That’s
my rule, Ellis, and I’m a man o’ my word. Now,
ye know, my man—a lash for a pen’orth with a
minimum of twenty, I’m not vindictive —I might
.give a dozen, but I only give twenty. If you must
. RECORP'
.

(Apologies to PUCK, N. Y.)
steal —and once a thief always a thief is my
experience—for your own sake steal at least twenty-
pence worth, for you’ll never get a stripe less than
a score. Mark ye that!”
“ I do, sir !” Ellis sainted with the chill of a
fear biting into his heart, for —God help him !
lie must steal ! He knew it- he could not help
himself —and as Ovens had been so explicit, his
flesh crept in anticipation of the floggings to
come.
“ Not vindictive!” he cried to himself as he set
about his first job in his master’s stabloyard.
“ He'll only give twenty, when he might give a
dozen. He’s a joking devil, he is, as if a score
ain’t more than a dozen.”
Ho was right enough in affirming that Ovens
was a joking devil, but, as a new-arrival, he bad
yet to learn that at Botany Bay a dozen (of lashes)
included five more than a score.
# # *
The manner of his learning ifc was as
follows :
For a fortnight he successfully struggled against
numerous temptations literally thrown in his way.
Loaves of bread, lumps of meat, fragments of
soap, even a flask of rum, had been frequently
placed to his hand, to say nothing of the hundred
and one articles of a stable and harness-room equip-
ment which it was possible for him to appropriate
and sell. And with respect to all and each he had
remained honest.
But, one Sunday morning, shortly after the
dawn of a spring day sun, penetrating through
tho cracks in the weatherboards of the stable-loft,
had chased the drowsiness from his eyes, Ellis
arose —and fell. Beside him, on the deck of the
loft, and within a foot of the straw-filled tick,
which, unsupported by a bedstead, served him for
a resting-place, lay a plug of tobacco. It was not
in the convict nature to resist that bribe. In half
the time it takes to pen the words descriptive of
the action, a finger’s-breadth of the weed was in
his mouth.
“ Ha, ha !” laughed a merry-toned voice. “ Ho,
ho, my man ! Better chew the whole plug now.
It’s a dozen I’ll give you for the plug, and twenty
for the bit, so, if I were you, I’d take the lot.”
The voice was that of Major John Ovens,
Acting-Chief Engineer, bosom friend of Judge-
Advocate Bent, and trusted functionary of His
Britannic Majesty and H.B.M.’s satraps Brisbane
and Darling. And it proceeded from a corner of
the loft where, under a heap of warm hay, that
worthy gentleman had ensconced himself.
“I’ll teach you to be honest, my man ! Nobody
shall stay in my employ unless he’s honest. And
you’re going to stay in my employ. You don t
like me, my man, and that's the reason I’m going
to keep you. I always keep a man who hates me.
I break him or make him.”
“ Are you a—man—sir ? ” stammered the
transport.
“Ha, ha! good fellow! Not a man merely,
but an officer, sir, and a gentleman, by virtue of
His Majesty’s Commission—ha, ha ! ”
“ By the (Commission of Beelzebub, you mean !’
cried the maddened convict, who was not as yet
used to Major Ovens’ playful little way of treating
everything, even human backs and hearts, as a
joke. “You’re a devil, sir—and a spy—a —
peaching cull, sir.”
Ovens had drawn himself erect, and smiling the
while, was plucking the straws from the shabby
undress jumper and trowsers he had worn as his
night-gear. But Ellis’ reckless words banished
the smile. There was a grim fact in his career of
which the term “spy” unpleasantly reminded.
His voice cooled to the cuttingest of accents as he
rejoined.
“ There, that will do. Polish the harness, get
your breakfast and prepare for whipping at eight
o’clock. Are you a raw ’un ? ”
“ What’s that ? And you can’t flog me without
bringing me before a magistrate!”
“Tut, tut, can’t I? I’m a magistrate. Why,
7could hang you. There’s only one other man
besides the Governor who could do that straight
off! Would you like me to try ?”
The pleasant temper of the Major was re-
asserting itself. He was becoming jocose again.
Still, though approaching jocularity, he was
speaking the truth. Johnston of Annandale and
himself could hang without trial. We will tell
you some day bow tin's power of high justice was
conferred and exorcised.
Transport Ellis was deceived by the Major’s
resumed jocoseness of tone. He thought he was
not so offended as he seemed. So, though his
every nerve was quivering with the fear of the
threatened punishment, he, too, Iried to smile.
“Try, if you like. I’d as lief be hanged as
whipped, or lead this dog’s life.”
“Gad, I will try! Go down, pris’ner, to the
yard and prepare for death—h’m —hanging.”
“God ! d'ye mean it, sir?”
“I’m a man of my word as I’ve already told
you, pris’ner. Go down ! ” He pushed Ellis
down the ladder, and the poor wretch, thoroughly
unnerved, fell at its foot.
“ Get up ! ” ordered Ovens. Ellis’ only response
was a moan. His head beat on the cobble-stones,
two or three feet inside the stable-door.
Ovens passed out.
* # #
An English oak—planted, so the legend
ran, by Captain King—was burgeoning its twenty
years’ growth of trunk and limb with the symbols
of the new Spring. In the Autumns, its leaves
fell on the gables of the stable, so close was it to
the building. Only, one limb stretched out, four-
teen feet from the ground, over the carriage path-
way.
Ovens whistled. Half-a dozen heads protruded
from as many windows or doors.
“ Here, Mottram—Pike—Hughes, reeve a rope
on the gallows-tree and stand by to haul. Pris’ner
Ellis here does not think I’ve power to scrag him.”
With hands at the salute as they ran, the three
assigned men named hurried to the stable.
There was an expertness about their movements
which betokened a shameful familiarity with the
routine of the ceremony.
One hauled from under the eaves a long ladder,
which he placed against the projecting arm of the
oak.
A second pulled from a locker in the stable a
tortuous length of rope. The yarn was stained in
places.
The third, as tlje rope was unwound, lifted an
end of it, ran up the ladder, and, more slowly,
moved along the limb. Half its length he paused;
for there hung, by an iron clamp, a pulley-block.
He passed the rope on the sheave. “ Will that do,
y’r Honor ?” he respectfully asked. The Major
nodded affirmatively. Of course it would do!
Why shouldn’t it ? Had not Hughes reeved tho
rope once or twice before, and, therefore, did he
not know all about the way of doing it ?
The rope-ends dangled, and swung loosely in
the breeze. The noosed end was tho first to still
in its pendulous movement.
“ Now, Ellis, say a prayer, and get ready !”
The fellow, with his head bent between his arms
on the cobble-stones, shook, but did not answer.
“ Get up, Ellis !” Thus Ovens ordered. Still,
no answer.
“ Draw him up !”
Mottram hastened to fling the noose round the
transport’s neck.
Ovens turned to the others. “At the word
haul! ” he said ; but as he uttered the devilish
speech, he exchanged a meaning look with them.
They raised their hands in salute and to signify
they understood. They clasped, one behind the
other, the rope.
“One!” exclaimed the torturer —otherwise the
officer and gentleman.
Mottram and Pike pulled. They hauled Ellis
to his feet —and at the uplifting of the Major’s
hand stopped. Ellis staggered, reeled, thrust his
fingers between the hemp and his neck.
“ Devil!” he gasped.
“ But the Devil isn’t as black as he’s painted !”
said Owens. “P’r’apsthis one isn’t as black as
you think !” He laughed ; he was quite merry
again, quite.
, “What—are you—hanging me for?” the victim
of the torture spluttered, with foam urging itself
between the phrases.
“For fun !” whispered Hughes, who was stand-
ing close to him. “ Shut up. He’ll ’a done soon.”
“ For larceny !” said Ovens.
* * •
Witn eye and ear intent upon his master,
Ellis had not heard the syllables sympathetically
muttered by his fellow-servant. He heard but the
Major’s voice.
“ Larceny!” With a piteous hoarseness he
enunciated the word. The rope was being
tautened,
“Larceny of one plug of tobacco !”
“ Larceny he stopped to get his fingers once
more between the rope and his flesh. “ Larceny !
Then—l—pray—the benefit of clergy.”
He flung himself on the ground ; on his knees ;
his sudden impulse had pulled the line through
the fingers of the unofficial hangmen.
“I—pray—benefit—of clergy !” Ovens mimicked
the speech, and then burst into a roar of laughter.
The joke was rich, superb !
“ Yes, I pray benefit of clergy ! I did at home
—and it saved me !”
The Major, palpitating with his merriment,
assumed once more a sternness of voice.
“ You’ve prayed it once, then ? Don’t ye know
you fool, that you can’t pray it a second
time ! Get ready there again !”
Pike and Mottram tightened their grip.
“Two!”
Once more the “ Condemned ” stood on his
feet—if a reeling position, only maintained to a
degree of crectness by the haulage, could be termed
standing.
“No last dying speech and confession, Ellis?
None? Then (to the men) Three, and a sling. ’
The creatures hauled Ellis swung Ir'gh—re-
volved once, twice— and in obedience to a down-
ward wave of the Major’s hand, was allowed to
run down to the earth again. He fell in a
writhing, groaning, gesticulating heap.
• * •
As for Major Ovens, he was, to use the
expression he himself employed when he told the
story that same afternoon at his own dinner-table,
in “ fits of laughter.”
“The poor sinner actually thought I intended
tie hang him then and there as 1 did ” He
stopped in his recital. The younger generation of
“ free ” colonists was represented at the table and
perhaps that ugly story had better be left untold.
“And what did you do, then? Flog him, of
course ? ” questioned a guest.
“ Well, I couldn’t let the theft of the tobacco
go unpunished. I gave him a dozen—the fool
thought a dozen was tw'elve only.”
“ And what did he do? ”
“Do! Nothing! But he said ”
f “ What, sir ! ”
“ That I’d never rest in my grave. That if no
one else stole my body from its coffin he would ! ”
* # •
On Monday, May 21st, 1827, an
attempt, led by a convict who had been nick-
named “Benefit o’ Clargy Bill,” was made to
remove the remains of the late Major Ovens from
the vault, contiguous to the tomb of Judge-
Advocate Bart, at Garden Island, in which they
were deposited.
The depredators, as Mr. Robert Howe termed
them in the Sydney Gazette, were unsuccessful
in their main object, through a circumstance here-
inafter to be related.
Nevertheless, the prophecy of transport Ellis,
otherwise “ Benefit o’Clargy Bill,” was fulfilled.
Major Ovens’ remains were not allowed to rest on
Garden Island. They repose in St. Leonard’s
Cemetery, North Shore. But the authorities of
the New Regime who ordered their exhumation
and transfer were not, it is to be presumed,
informed of the act of desecration which
accompanied the removal.
Which act had clearly its origin in the pro-
ceedings of that Sunday morning.

The Prince of Wales is heavily interested
in trade. He is the most important shareholder
in a leading London hotel, and has put much
money into and used his influence extremely on
behalf of two big shops.
Dean O’Hea said to a recent interviewer; —
“No man was ever more misrepresented than
John Price, of Pentridge. He was an eminently
just man, firm, but never cruel, and exactly fitted
for the position he held.”
The Abbott family, of N.S.W., are fairly
mixed up with the shearing trouble. The epistles
of W. E. Abbott forced the pastoralists, under
the screw of the Jews, into a situation in which
J. P. Abbott bobs up as mediator.
When the late Dr. Beaney first made his
will, he bequeathed Dr. Fulton £25 to buy a
mourning ring. But professional affection is not
all-enduring. In a codicil he revoked the bequest
—didn’t even “save him a drink.”
Millionaire Baronet Clarke retires from
the V.R.O. stewardship, and, it is said, from the
turf. The bookmakers only will feel sorry. The
Bart, never owned a big winner, though he owns
some of the finest equine blood in the country.

committed by Mr. Parnell has finally cut him off
from the Roman ecclesiastics. Hence one finds

by the remark that the latter’a recent marriage
“ was no marriage.”

“ Silence ! This is not a theatre ! ” such
was Chief Justice Coleridge’s check to the
applause after the speech of Cumming’s barrister.
But a party at the back said coolly, “ You have
made it so ’’—for there were a score of fashionable
women on the Bench. Edmund Yates slates
Coleridge flayfully, and says he has a document
which can make him wink. We believe this refers
to an alleged breach of promise.
F. Anstey, author of “ Vice Versa,” lately
bought in Baris a book in French, which he
recognised as a straight-out translation of one of
his own works. Ho wrote to the French
“author” praising the book highly and asking
leave to translate it into English. The modest
Frenchman replied that he feared the volume did
not deserve Mr. Anstey’s great praise, and that he
had already published an English translation.
Premier Munro’s monotonous declaration
that he has “ worked harder than any of them ”
(the unemployed) is based upon the dim and dis-
tant fact that he once pulled a press. A specimen
of Munro’s work hangs framed against the wall
of a Melbourne printing-office. It isn’t much for a
man to go blowing about whenever the question
of manual labour crops up. But it is something.
Tlie only specimen of Duncan Gillies’ work that
lias attained to the dignity of a frame is the penny
he lavished upon a waiter, some four or five years
ago.
President Kruger, of the Transvaal Re-
public, was invited to attend a ball in honour of
Queen Victoria’s last -birthday. His secretary
forwarded a reply to the effect that Kruger “con-
siders a bail as Baal’s service, for which reasons
the Lord ordered Moses to kill all the offenders.
Whereas such is, consequently, contrary to His
Honor’s principles, His Honor cannot consent to
the misuse of his name in connection with such a
ball.”
In the diaiy of the late Commodore Good-
enough, it is related that, walking down one of
the streets of a Chinese town one day, the commo-
dore saw two mendispujing violently. Suddenly
they began to fight. One caught the other by the
pigtail, and, holding him firmly, cut his head
clean off his shoulders with a large knife. He
flung the head' in the street, and deliberately
walked away, none of the bystanders attempting
to interfere with him.
Old Mike L , a wealthy Melbourneite,
went to an artist, a dab in oils, for a picture of
“me house, wid meself and phamilee sittifi’ in
front.” The job was done, and Mick didn’t
object to £250, but “ Stay, Misther, fwhat's this ?
Ye’ve painted me wid only one oye.” “Profile,
my dear sir, profile.” “ Profeel be —— 1 I won’t
be painted wid wan oye. Put in the other, and
here’s your money.” The upshot was a county
court case, when the artist won, of course.

At a dinner party last January Sir William
Gordon Gumming was appointed to take Lady N.
into dinner (she had heard that he had said he
hoped that when her husband died a great friend
of hers would “make an honest woman of her.”)
When he approached her, therefore, in the crowded
drawing-room she burst into hysterical tears and
cried out loudly that it was a shame to send her
into dinner with a man u'ho cheated at cards. This
created an extraordinary sensation, and was the
first public broaching of the scandal which every-
body at Tranby-Croft had agreed to keep quiet.
There is little doubt, remarks one of
the best-informed of English newspaper-cor-
respondents, that Mrs. Lycett Green before her
marriage was very fond of Sir William Gordon-
Cumming, and it was reported after the ceremony
that Sir William had boasted that he could have
married the lady, but would not. Eor this
reason neither the husband of the lady nor her
family would spare the alleged slanderer of her
good name. Gumming has always been a great
masher, and has not always held his tongue about
his alleged conquests, many of which were no
doubt imaginary.
The jamming into the peerage of Sir
George Stephen, of Canada, is regarded by know-
ing people in England as the first fruits of ex-
Governor Carington’s stupid suggestion for heating
up colonial loyalty by instituting a colonial life-
peerage. George Stephen has fallen from a self-
won position to the debasing level of a Court
toady. In 1850 he landed in Canada from Scot-
land, with nothing but strong arms and a parsi-
monious disposition. He joined a dry goods store
at Montreal, and in 12 years became head, of the
firm, from which position he swelled into an
opulent merchant, and became President of the
Bank of Montreal, and railway financier. He is
62 years old.
G. R. Sims, in the Referee, tells a good
yarn about himself and his haberdasher. For
years, says G, R. S., he has bought his ties and
gloves at a certain shop, paying cash down and
carrying away his purchases in his pocket. The
other day he found himself short of coin and asked
for his little parcel to bo sent home with the bill
enclosed. It duly arrived, addressed Sims Reeves,
Esq. Then “ Dagonet ” called at the shop and
explained that he wasn’t the septuagenarian tenor,
nor a bit like him. His next parcel arrived for
“ Reeves, Esq. ” —the shopkeeper having evi-
dently forgotten which of the two names his cus-
tomer had repudiated. “And this is fame!”
says poor Sims.
Emperor William lias just given the world
another manifestation of unfilial behaviour. - A
short time ago the rector of his parish of Kockern,
on the Rhine, was sentenced to a term of im-
prisonment for refusing to administer the sacra-
ments to a dying man until a photograph of the
Empress Frederick, the young monarch’s mother,
was removed from the bedroom. His pretext was
that the empress was in a dress that was too
decollete, and showed too much of the imperial
and somewhat voluminous bust. On the matter
being brought to the emperor’s notice, he demanded
to see the portrait of his mother which had given
offence to the priest. As soon as it was placed
before him, he gave orders for the immediate
liberation of the prisoner.
This, from the Pall Mall Gazette, is
substantially true:—
The first R.C. priest to arrive on Bendigo in ’5l was
Rev. Dr. Backhaus, a young and energetic German, with a
doctor’s degree in both divinity and medicine. He prac-
tised both professions, and naturally accumulated wealth
at a time when the smallest services were remunerattd
with nuggets. In time he became the owner of a local
brewery, a score of hotels, a station of 50,000 acres, two
vineyards, and a large amount of house property. Such
was the state of things in 1874 when the late Pope resolved
on erecting Bendigo into a bishopric, and declared that
Dr, Backhaus (then Dean) should not receive a mitre
until he severed himself from secular pursuits. That the
doctor declined to do, whereupon the Pope promptly
nominated Dr. Crane. Dr. Backhaus left his beloved
Bendigo, and retired to a mansion near Melbourne. He
died five years ago, bequeathing £250,000 to the diocese
of Bendigo, but ordering that it should be at interest for
20 years from the day of his death, apparently in oi*der to
baulk the present bishop.
There is at least one colonial precedent for
Postmaster-General O’Connor holding office with-
out being elected to Parliament by the people.
When “Dick” Ireland, Q.C., was Attorney-
General in the Victorian “ People’s ” Ministry—
Richard Heales, Premier—he failed on three dis-
tinct occasions to obtain a seat. On the third
effort, upon some informality he appealed to the
E. and Q. committee who decided that the sitting
member was not duly elected, but would not say
whether Ireland should have the seat or whether a
fresh election should take place. The matter was
referred to the House, Heales moving that the
blank in the writ be filled up with Ireland’s name.
This was fiercely opposed by J. D. Wood and
others, but finally carried and Ireland took his
seat. During that Parliament Wood always spoke
of Ireland as the “Honorable Member for the
House.”

remedy.

Society
In the N-S.W. Assembly, the other night,
Edden, miners’ member, threw a blaze of light
upon the social state of a married miner. “ After
bringing up a family, and working in the mines
for 30 years, if everything were squared up I
should be ll|d. short of a shilling.”
# • *
Miss Grace Mackie, defendant in the great
M’Meckan Melbourne will-case, is a strong-
minded, shrewd, hard-headed Scotch lady. But
she is very female, for all this. Not all her strong-
mindedness, shrewdness, hard-headedness, and
Scotchness are proof against her feminine disin-
clination to own up to her real age. When
Burves, Q.C., had her on in cross-examination
frill, she stood the fire bravely, until the astute
counsellor, up to this time unable to make her
wobble, wanted to know her age. Then, all the
influence of the oath, as administered solemnly
by the associate, got weak-kneed, and Miss
Grace, aged 52, wanted to knock off five years,
right away. The effort, however, was palpable,
and the barrister saw through it. So he put on
the torture properly, and Miss Mackie had to cry
neccavi, and sue for pardon. Then Purves was
lovely. “Not from me,” solemn’y cried the
ingenuous lawyer, raising his eyes towards
Heaven, “ must you ask pardon, but from a Higher
Source.” It was bathos, and a smile, a yard wide,
settled on the countenances of those who were in
the know ; but the jury looked grave, and the
stroke went home, for you could easily see that
Miss Mackie had been brought up verr.a piously
indeed. But she had proved she was a woman.
* * *
This is verbatim : —Purves, Q.C. ; “What
was your age when you came to the colony ? ”
“ About 20.” “ I don’t want to press you unduly,
but is that a lady’s answer or a truthful one? ”
“ A lady’s answer.” “A lady’s answer; then it
is not true ! ” “No.”

“If it had not been for beer,” says John
Hancock, M.L.A., “many Melbourne workmen
would have been starved during the past year.
The only meal they could get was the free lunch
at the publicans’ counter.” Free lunch isn’t food
—it’s only bait. The flies don’t ultimately fatten
on fly-papers.
# # #
A young and lovely Southern wife has had
a year in Europe under queer circumstances. She
was caught in flagrante delicto, but merely defied
her hubby, saying she could prove that he was
carrying on naughtily with a certain wealthy
lady who belonged to somebody else, besides “ so-
and-so, and so-and-so.” Result: Instead of a
writ for divorce, a fine fat cheque and a merry
trip to Hingland. She is now returning, and
when she does arrive, she will find hubby “ pro-
tecting ” a bewchus actress.

# * *
The Want-Ray anecdote recalls to a Bul-
letin correspondent an incident in the life of A.
G. Taylor. While representing Mudgee in the
N.S W. Assembly that long but epigrammatic
youth passed a good deal of his bachelorhood in
Sydney, and was picked up one night by a gentle-
man who thought the Giraffe needed “ protec-
tion.” He brought him home to North Shore,
looked after him, and at the breakfast-table next
morning introduced him to the family. The lady
of tlie house inilitely hoped that Mr. Taylor
would in future take lietter care of himself, as
Ids “constituents at Mudgee might find fault
with him.” Judge of the family surprise when it
was found that the Taylor who hat! been cared for
over night was not “ Mudgee ” Taylor, at all, but
another of the satin name from a town further out
towards the baek-blocks. How is that for a
“strange coincidence ?”
One of Sydney’s best, if not the best,
domino and card-players is the walking image of
the Prince of Wales. Figure, bald-head, features,
and beard are all there, but the writer would put
his “ bit ” on the Sydney man if the pair met and
give Tummy the “ throw-in ” of his own gambling-
kit.
# # *
The sudden “ departure ” of that “ fly ”
pair from Wynyard-square, Sydney, reminds us
of one A , a butcher, in a very large way of
business in Melbourne. He at one time failed for
a paltry hundred or so, and was looked upon—as
he pathetically said— as “ nobody.” “ Next time
I go,” he would say, “I’ll make some of their
heads ache.” When getting near the end of his
rope, he one day attended a big cattle-sale and
had a “mob” knocked down to him. “Cash,
please, Mr. A .” “ Oh, no ; the usual terms.”
“No ; cash down.” “Well, here’s my cheque.”
“ Won’t take it.” “Not take my cheque ! Why,
you’ll be refusing the cheques of these gentlemen
next,” pointing to George Petty and T. K. Ben-
nett, who stood by. “Oh, no,” quoth the
auctioneer. “I’ll take their cheques for any
amount.” “All right. Here, Petty, just swap
cheques with me.” And Petty did, for a very big
amount, and regretted it till the day of his death.
Within 48 hours A had cleared out, (leaving
Dick Goldsbrough, Petty, and s.ores of others
lamenting his defalcations, amounting to many
thousands, and, strange to say, he was never
heard of afterwards.

“ At yesterday's sitting of the Wesleyan

many scandals which have lately arisen in aristo-
cratic circles, and in which the Prince of Wales,
highly-placed army officers, and members erf Par-

Did Nelson say to Hardy what it is
traditionally reported he said? The last chapter
of the discussion goes to confirm the tradition,
and to show that the man Capper, who heard from
Fletcher, whose master was told by Admiral
Smythe, who knew Hardy, “that Nelson never
spoke after he was wounded,” is simply in the last
stage of senile forgetfulness, or in the first exercise
of his faculty of fiction-writing. A Colonel
Henderson, a son of a niece of Beatty, surgeon of
the Victory, possesses the doctor’s narrative of
Nelson’s closing hours. This paper was read by
Hardy twice, and as Southey derived his account
of Nelson’s last words from it, it is safe to say
that the dying seaman’s commendation of Lady
Hamilton and his daughter to the nation, is
authentic. By-the-way, Captain Crawford Pasco,
of Queenscliff, Vic., the son of Nelson’s Flag-
Lieutenant, who hoisted the famous “duty
signal,” should have something to say on this
matter. Over his mantel at Queenscliff hangs a
letter from his father, dated from on board the
Victory at Portsmouth. It is framed in Victory
timber. Pa°co, pere, brought Governor Mac-
quarie to N.S.W. He beat to quarters just
before entering the Heads, and every gun was
shotted. Macquarie expected to have to fight the
Johnstone-Macarthur crowd.

The N.S.W. Assembly, during the no-
confidence debate, was graced in the afternoons
with Lady Jersey’s presence, and in the evenings
with Lady Parkes’. Those distinguished per-
sonages (of course at different hours) occupied the
self-same chair behind the “bar.” Sir Henry has
whined in the press that he would have accepted a
defeat cheerfully ; but the whining after the event
won’t dovetail in with the presence of Lady
Parkes behind the bar right through that
miserable 23-hours’sitting. The face of Sir Henry
was by no means a pleasing picture to look upon :
he looked deeply harassed under the punctures of
the barbed wire which the Labor-party has
tin-own round him ; while his spouse seemed to bo
absolutely disconsolate except when her attention
was drawn away from that coming “ breadless
home ” by the witticisms of Jack Want, Q.C.

pence—(shall we stop here? No, go on.)—but she
turned it into a shilling.

Dear Bulletin, — Apropos your paragraph
about Spiridion Candiottis. In 1862 Candiottis
was one of the best-known characters on the
Lachlan goldfields. He had a dwelling-house and
dispensary in Rankin-street, Forbes, just above
the Standard Theatre. The doctor's special
hobby was anatomy, and he narrowly missed a
serious scrape while pursuing his favourite task.
A young fellow of fine physique having died of
fever, the doctor coveted his remains and tried to
fiurchase them, but without success. He there-
ore determined to resurrect the subject, and one
dark night succeeded in doing so, and boro his
prize home in triumph to his surgery. This some-
how became suspected, and the unworkmanlike
manner in which the theft at the cemetery had
been carried out lending confirmation to the
rumour that the grave had been robbed, it was
opened and the coffin found minus of its contents.
The doctor’s premises were at once raided by the
police, and he was caught red-handed in the act of
boiling the subject in a copper he had rigged up in
his kitchen, his object being to remove all the
flesh from the bones and retain the skeleton in-
tact. The matter was hushed up somehow; at any
rate it never came before the courts. There was
at this time a storekeeper on the Lachlan named
Cowen, or Scowen, but better known to the
diggers as “Old Kerosene.” This worthy was
trapped by the doctor one evening making hot
love to an actress in whom he was interested, and
No more the Papuan who would he a tint)

Round the Shops and Shows.
Mr. C. Corbett has opened at 35 Park-
street an establishment for making shirts to order.
Of course shirts can be bought ready-made any-
where, but was there ever a ready-made shirt yet
which proyed a fit? A man with a large neck and
short arms finds himself with about a foot too
much sleeve, whilst a tall, slight man has his cuffs
round his elbows, not to mention other well-known
discomforts. Mr. Corbett guarantees the best value
in material and workmanship, at a cost of 7s. fid.
to 9s. fid. each shirt. Repairing is another
specialty at this establishment.
* * #
We strongly recommend those of our lady
readers who have not already done so to make a
trial for themselves of a new soap lately introduced
into the Sydney market. Tiiis is known as
“ Macfie’s Cold Water Soap ” for washing clothes.
We hear on all sides of its ivonderful efficiency,
and its merits as a labor-saver, to say nothing of
the economy in time and fuel, so it must prove a
perfect boon to hard-working housewives in re-
lieving them of many a weary day’s rubbing,
tubbing, and scrubbing. The principal ingredients
or this soap have long been in use at most of the
large laundries, but nothing of a similar nature
has ever been offered to the public tcfore in such
an attractive and cheap form.
# # •
Jewellery, as well as dresses and bonnets,
is subject to the freaks of fashion, in a minor
degree, perhaps, but the fact cannot be disputed
that there is such a thing as “old fashioned ”
jewellery. This used to mean one of two things—
either the old jewels had to be discarded in place
of modern ones, or old-fashioned rings, bracelets,
or brooches had to be worn. The former plan
needed well-lined pockets, but as all cannot boast
of such a happy state of affairs, it will doubtless
be interesting to know that Mr. Leon Harris, of
Market-street (next Hunter’s Boot Pala.e) re-
models old-fashioned jewellery in the most artistic
fashion. No need to wear old-fashioned jewels,
ladies.
* * *
A wise man eats to live, whilst a fool lives
to eat; but the man who dines at the City Buffet,
whatever his motive for eating may be, is cer-
tainly no fool—he knows where he ean obtain a
really good dinner. The ladies’ room, which
opens off George-street, is pronounced by them to
be “the best ladies’ dining-room in Sydney.” The
menu is of the choicest—the appointments and
attendance first-class.

KILLER.
294 Castleresgh-street, June 25, 1891.
Radam’s Microbe Killer 00., Gentlemen, —For
forty years I have been a sufferer from rheu-
matism, and latterly in the most aggravated form,
often having acute attacks which made me almost
helpless for days, insomuch that it was with the
greatest pain and difficulty that I could move
about at all, and only able to get up stairs on my
hands and knees. A friend who had tried the
“ Microbe Killer ” with beneficial results sent me
a jar of the medicine, which I took, and I have
now taken three jars of “Microbe Killer” and
the result has been a great wonder to myself and
my friends, for now I can get about freely, with-
out pain. Igo up and down stairs without any
trouble, and I am greatly improved in health
every way. In fact my suffering from rheumatism
seems a thing of the past, and I can recommend
the “ Microbe Killer ” to anyone with confidence,
knowing what it has done for me. Several have
tried your medicine on my recommendation for
other diseases—and one a cancer patient—with
the same beneficial results. —Yours respectfully,
MRS. WHITAKER.

fhere was an awful row in consequence. The
doctor challenged “Old Kerosene” to fight
with either swords or pistols. Ihe chal-
lenge was accepted, but the police inter-
fered to prevent the duel. The races were
on at the time, and on one of the three days
of the meeting, a fight was arranged in the ortho-
dox English fashion, with fists. The fight came
off in the scrub, about a quarter of a mile from
the grand-stand, I was there and saw it all. I
do not recollect who was the doctor’s second, but
Edmund Holloway, the actor, officiated for “ Old
Kerosene.” There were about 300 diggers present,
two or three troopers, and Captain Brown, the
o-old-commissioner. Both the combatants stripped
to the waist and went at it, hammer and tongs.
They were both very stout men, and in any-
thing but good fighting condition. The con-
sequence was that, after the first round, they
puffed and panted like a couple of porpoises.
They, however, stuck to it manfully, and
pounded one another in fine style, stimulated
by the encouraging cheers of the spectators. In
the second round the doctor succeeded in giving
Old Kerosene “a stinger on the boko,” which
tapped his claret, and caused great excitement in
the crowd. “Go it, Candy, hit him again!”
“Now then, Kerosene, walk into him,” and such
like exclamations, accompanied by a volley of the
choicest expletives, were heard on all sides. The
fight did not last long, however, for after five or
six rounds both men were so completely exhausted
that their friends persuaded them to make it a
draw and cry quits. The doctor had a black eye,
and both had a few scratches on the body, caused
by tumbling about in the scrub. They returned
to the course and shook hands over a bottle of fizz.
I have seen none of the parties since 1862, except
Holloway, who is playing in Dampier’s Company
in Melbourne ■ just now.— Trotter Bethune,
Moonee Ponds, Vic., July 20, 1891.

_# * *
Some years ago a wealthy Adelaide lady
went to the late Dr. Beaney and said that her
husband, Moneybags, had for years annoyed her-
self and family with his heart-disease. He had
unavailingly consulted many leading doctors—
could Beaney do anything for him? As a result
Moneybags appeared before the diamond doctor
to explain his troubles. Nobody, he said,
understood his case. Beaney looked very wise,
told the old hunks that his heart had
shifted four inches, and, without warning, struck
him a shower of blows on the chest. “Thats
done it !” shouted the doctor. And so old Money-
bags believed until the day of his death— f rom
spinal shook through a fall downstairs.
* # «
When a fashionable funeral is on now in
Melbourne, and the mourners are gathering in the
mansion of the defunct, a strange woman gener-
ally drops in, dressed in deep black and weeping
bitterly. Nobody no ices her as a rule, or asks
her any questions. If she is observed, her pro-
found grief leads to the conviction that she is a
poor relation of the deceased, or some humble
individual to whom he had been a father and a
benefactor in thi-t vale of tears. She never says
anything, but sobs in a heart-rending manner in
a corner, and when the proceedings are over
about £3O worth of portable property is usually
missing.
* * *
A bright and sparkling matinee entertain-
ment is now running in Russell-street, Melbourne,
to really good business. The show consists in
getting into the watch-house on soms pretext
cr other, and listening beside the wall
which separates that building from the gaol,
while Murderer Colston clanks about in his
chains in the yard on the other side. The
condemned lunatic is loaded down with about a
crate of hardware, and it bangs and shakes and
jingles around him as he wanders back and for-
ward, puffing his pipe.

bidding a magistrate to act as a secretary to
either a pastoralists’ or a shearers’ union, ought he
not, in common fairness, to prevent bank-managers
out west from adjudicating in “ labour ” cases, as

toralists ?
* * *
At Gundabooka station (N.S.W.) last
week there were more police than shearers: 35
police were protecting 34 shearers. Although only
three sheds were shearing, 300 police were intro-
duced into the Bourke district. The district com-
prises 200 sheds, taking from 20 to 60 shearers
each. Now, if 300 police are essential to protect
three sheds, how many will it require for the
remaining 197. sheds ?

A washed-out sufferer sent in, amongst
other items of losses on which he based his claim
to bo relieved out of the Melbourne Flood Relief
Fund, the following:—“My wife’s silk dress,
value, 20 guineas ; dress coat which I bought for
the Mayor's ball three years ago (say) £5.”
# # #
A weeping kleptomaniac turned up at
Malmesbury, Vic., the other day. He told the
court that he was the son of an English clergy-
man—they are all the sons and daughters of
clergymen—that his disease was hereditary, and
that he had been sent out from Hingland on
account of it. He blubbed a Niagara and asked
the court to shoot him at once for the disgrace he
was. He got bail, however, and now all the
large-limbed police in the district are turning over
logs and dragging rivers and invading churches
looking for him.
* # *
It has been ascertained that the defalca-
tions of G. E. Labertouche, ex-paymaster of
Imperial pensioners at Sydney, amount to nearly
£12,000, and have extended over 12 years.

M‘Meckan “will” case, Melbourne. “Are
you aware that anyone had access to the deceased
immediately before his demise?” “Certainly
not. No one had axes to him to my knowledge.
He died a natural death.”
#■ * #
The trusting Sims, M.L.A. of Adelaide,
is not enjoying his trip “home.” At Brighton
(Eng.) races he backed a winner for £lO and found
himself welshed, and while he hunted around for
t he missing villain, his gorgeous hundred-guinea
timepiece was quietly annexed by the “boys.”
It will be interesting to hear Sims snort at British
institutions when he comes back minus his watch,
and loaded up with winning tickets issued by
gentlemen who have no sense of honour.

Editor Bulletin. — Sir, —In justice to a young
clergyman in our district, allow me space for contradic-
tion of statements copied by you from a newspaper called
Tin: Presbyterian, which are in effect as follows :—l.
That a person dying at Moree (two months and more ago),
being an Independent in religion, the clergyman at Moree
refused to read any service at the grave. Such is one
statement. This is the fact; that the clergyman referred
to was not asked for, nor were his services desired, by the
friends of the deceased. It is true that besides myself,
there were, including the curate (incumbent) three clergy-
men of the Church of England in Moree on the Sunday on
which the funeral took place, but no one of us was asked
to take any part in the ceremony, consequently none of us
refused to do what we were not asked to do. As for the
assertion that a plea of the ground being unconsecrated
was given as a reason for not reading any service at the
grave in question, it is simply an unmitigated falsehood—
I can call it nothing else. There is no part of the ceme-
tery belonging to any body of Christians at Moree which
is consecrated, and the clergyman of the Church of Eng-
land assures me that he has several times officiated at
funerals of persons not members of the Church of England
in parts of the cemetery not assigned to our communion,
and that he is perfectly aware, and teas perfectly aware,
that no consecrated ground exists at Moree. Steps are
being taken to find out and punish the propagator of this
libel, which is malicious in both a legal and moral sense,
and which at the time was contradicted in the local press
by the incumbent and myself, as also in one or two news-
papers'in Sydney in which it had been set forth.— Jas. F.
Turner, Bp. of Grafton and Armidale.
* * *
The latest in wedding presents comes from
W.A. No, it wasn’t a £20,000 cheque. The
daughter of the riding-ganger of one of the Public
Works contracts married the possessor of a patch
of land called by courtesy a farm. To encourage
the young people and show that there was no
ill-feeling, the girl’s father presented the bride-
groom with—2o loads of stable-manure. Well, it
was more useful than the usual electro-plated fish-
slice.
* » «
Murderer-—Bigamist—Newspaper Editor
—such is the record of a lately-deceased Maori-
lander, and a few hunks from his career may be
instructive to people who feel inclined to go and
do likewise. The murder was committed in Eng-
land, in youthful blood, and owing to extenuating
circumstances (including wealthy relations), the
sentence was penal servitude for life. He behaved
well in gaol, and owing to this, and the extenuat-
ing circumstances aforesaid, he was prematurely
released, on condition that he went to “the
kawlinies.” Arrived in Maoriland, he naturally
took to running a palladium of liberty, in which
he exposed all sorts of minor scoundrels most
unsparingly. He seemed to have homicidal mania,
and on several occasions came near murdering his
wife. Once he set forth, with a loaded revolver,
intent on the gore of a rival editor, but was met
and judiciously filled drunk on the way, so that
his benevolent object was frustrated. It was only
after the death of this notable that the fact of his
bigamy came to light. On his relatives at Horae
being appealed to for aid to the widow and
orphans in the Land of the Moa and the Maori,
they replied, in effect: “What do you mean?
We have been supporting his wife and family
here ever since he first went to prison !”

In English law divorce can be obtained on
the husband’s petition : Adultery only. On the
wife’s petition; Incestuous adultery, bigamy
with aididtery, rape, bestiality, adultery with
cruelty, with desertion without reason-
able excuse forNtwo years or upwards, adultery
and desertion arising from non-compliance with a
decree for restitution of conjugal rights. On the
petition of either hatband or wife : Prior marriage
existing, mental unapundness, within prohibited
degrees, want of age, fraud, duress, defective for-
malities, impotency./

A LAST REQUEST.
(INSCRIBED TO DR. CREED, M.L.C.)
O ! let me lie, when I am dead,
Where ne’er a wattle green
Can wave its branches overhead,
Nor any grass be seen.
0 ! put me not where children play }
I know how they behave,
And want no boys to sneak away
The flowers from my grave.
’Tis not in quiet, verdant nook
I'd pitch my final camp,
And listen to a babbling brook
Which kept my coffin damp.
Let poets prate of stars above—
I could not slumber sound
If spoony pairs came making love
Upon my little mound.
A marble slab I don’t require,
Nor palings that will rust;
But bake my body in the fire,
Till nought is left but dust;
An urn’s the nicest “lonely bed”
(I’ve one cremated niece) —
O ! let me lie when I am dead
Upon the mantelpiece.

“ Bathurst” ; The Bulletin is an absolutely independent
paper, which owes allegiance to no individual, party, or
sect. It is no more responsible for the Parliamentary
utterances, actions, or opinions of any politician who may
be or who may have been connected with it than it is for
the utterances of General Booth or Pope Leo. No poli-
tician has any proprietary interest in The Bulletin, nor
does any politician or political party exercise over it con-
trol of any kind ; nor have its directors any personal
political ambition or desire to enter Parliament or to
grind the axe of any individual with any such ambition.
The strength of The Bulletin, as its conductors are
aware, lies in its complete independence, which has been
proved and will be carefully preserved.

A new diving device now being introduced
among the Northern pearlers is “ a sort of shield
or armour of copper, which fits inside the dress
and over the hips of the diver, and keeps the dress
from pressing upon the vital portions of the human
anatomy.” Stronger piping and the electric-lamp
are also being brought into vougue.
Redgrave, in his diary, relates how
Rogers, the poet, a most absent-minded man, told
an equally absented-minded friend of how, walking
in the Champs “I was met by an old
lady who stopped and, looking fixedly at me,
said, ‘Sir, isn’t your name Rogers?’ Pausing a
moment, he was interrupted by his friend, who
said, “ Well, and was it?’”

Gordon & Gotch’s “A B.C. Guide”—in-
corporating the New South Wales Bradshaw—for
August is to hand. This is a federal guide giving
every railway-station in Australia. The road
guides to each colony have been carefully revised,
and the time tables arranged on the simple and
effective American system. “Notes of the
Month,” a new feature in the Guide, deal with
the new lines opened in Victoria and Queensland.

Poor old “Poddy” Boyle, of Orange,
N.S.W., has bottomed his last claim, and the
news of his death will move stirring memories in
the minds of thousands of old Australian diggers.
This Donegal bhoy came to Australia in ’59 and
was one of the first on the Snowy River diggings
in ’GI. Next he tackled the Lachlan, N.S.W.,
and made a rise ; wandered on to the Macquarie;
anon, into Queensland to the Canal Creek dig-
gings ; then, made a pile on a trip through the
Isles of New Zealand, and across the Pacific to
Californy, oh ! Back again in N.S.W., he was in
the Gulgong rush, which he left when the Parkes
rush broke out. In 75, he settled in Orange and
took to pub-keeping, drawing his last cork and
breath in the Diggers’ Arms.

mn third for a minor two-year-old race at Goodwood?
The horse (it wasn’t Dan. himself who ran third) is
Er.«dish-bred, and can run first, last, or anywhere it
chooses without altering the fact that we don’t care a
straw The Austraiianly-untaxed Mr. Dan. Cooper's
doings on the British turf are becoming an infernal nuis-
ance°to the far-off country which produces his unearned
increment -which produces the good red gold he is
chucking away in a foreign country.

One of the Victorian Mounted Rifles expedition
has written over to say that Carbine could give two stone
to any English thoroughbred, and that the only real first-
class animal he (the warrior) saw at “home” was Mons.
Meg the Australian filly. Was it Anthony Trollope who
remarked a disposition to “ blow ?” By the way, Carbine
happens to be a full-blooded Britisher, foaled in Maori-
lancl‘ « # #
A Melbourne paper, calling itself The Sports-
man ignored Roberts’ entertainment at the Athenaeum,
for the simple reason, apparently, that the billiard-
champion had staked some money at the office of a rival
r.v. Meanwhile, this miserable mockery of what a
sporting paper might be was deluging the larrikins with
streams' of pugilistic drivel- fountains of ungrammatical
gush—concerning all the broken-nosed Bobs and Billys
who can induce a “club ” of infatuated jugginses to pay
for the privilege of seeing strong animals put to the worst
possible use under Heaven. Journalism can’t sink much
lower than The Sportsman’s three-column description of
“ How We Found Sullivan,” in the course of which ap-
pears this immortal observation of the Slugger’s, printed
n huge type: “ Walk Right Up Herb and Take a Drink.”
° * * •
The last week ortwohas been a gory timeforthose
sections of the Melbourne and Sydney publics which
delight in “game chickens,” and swallow the drivel of
sporting papers concerning what might have happened if
Jim had got fairly home with his “ terrible right” in the
first round, or Champion This hadn’t “clinched ’ instead
of trying to plug Champion That on the “old spot.”
Considering that Goddard knocked out Choynski in a
genuine fashion, the encounter between those two well-
grown gentlemen was more conducive to sporting in-
sanity than the “ battle of the light-weights” in Sydney,
decided by one light-weight stoushing the other light-
webdit when he was kneeling, and therefore getting him-
selMgnominiously defeated on a “foul.” But neither
"■reat'event was worth a-tenth part of the money sub-
scribed to the inevitable “purse,” or anted-up to the
equally inevitable “gate.” The cash expended upon
professional pugilism is sadly wasted, even though it may,
in some eases, be the means of putting the roof of a pub.
over the bullet-heads of heroes the bulk of whom would,
if left to earn a decent living, be tolerably certain to
settle down in a prison. The so-called courage and
endurance of the broken-nosed brotherhood must be
sporting fictions, for tbe simple reason that 49 out of
every 50 of them degenerate into loafers when their little
day is ended. Where’s the courage of the bloated, played-
out plugger who asks you to give him a dollar for old
acquaintance sake ? Is he “ game ”to look out for a job ?
As for the “ cleverness ” of these people—most sickening
of the sporting “gags” in sporting rags—well, look at
the heads and faces of the odd 49 out of 50, draw them
into conversation, consider them in all their bearings,
and then say whether it’s possible for such over-rated
animals to be clever at anything (fighting not excepted)
under the sun.
# * *
John L. Sullivan said in America that he was
“making barrels of money” out of the stage. One
moderate-sized barrel will, we are afraid, be enough to
hold all the money John will make out of the Australian
stage, if he is paid strictly according to results. John
may be a god in his own country, but in this beastly place
every larrikin firmly believes that there are three Aus-
tralians, at least, who are thirsting to knock the stuffing
out of Johnel, and that two of them would probably
succeed ; also, that he could find a policeman good enough
to take Johnel in tow if necessary. The only possible
chance of redemption which The Bulletin sees for “ the
Big Fellow,” is for him to induce Sara Bernhardt to piay
Cleopatra to his Antony. Failing this, he should have a
shy at Joe Goddard.
» * *
The advent of J. L. Sullivan reminds us of
Morrisey and Bob M'Laren. Old Ballarat diggers will
remember Bob when he kept the Glasgow Arms in the
Main Road. Bob. fought and beat the great Morrisey
in California when, as he used to say, he held his life in
his fist, as the ring was surrounded by men armed with
revolvers and nearly all in favour of the American. Bob
imported his brother Alfred—who was also a “ bit of a
dab ” with his fists and not half a bad singer under his
concert-hall name of “Arthur Romer.” Poor Bob died
of a broken-heart—a woman at the bottom of his troubles.
“ Arthur Romer ” hung himself in the cabin of the
Cheviot on the voyage to Sydney. Look back on your
life, dear friend. It is one long funeral.
* * *
A fight not on the programme lately came off
at a certain fashionable club. It was in the small hours
of a.m., after an exciting night, when a well-known
amateur boxer, to freshen things up, began Witting a new
arrival from the old country, and a perfect Johnnie in
appearance. Words ran high, and the end was four
rounds with the small gloves for a breakfast all round.
A well-known leader of the bar held the watch, a
“Professor” was the referee, and the seconds were—well,
nevermind. The affair lasted only two rounds, and the
Johnnie showed thecompany thathe, forone, was “no Jim
Smith.” In the words of a certain fat gentleman; “By
, it was a take down.”
* # #
Melbourne has just sprouted a few typical
welching cases. Police Magistrate Panton said, with an
evident knowledge of the gambling world, that the
welchers’ victims were as despicable and blameworthy as
the welchers. The fact is that each party to any such a
transaction is trying, if not to diddle the other, to make
unearned money. One victim met his victimiser “ after
many days ” in a public thoroughfare, and, very natu-
rally, there were “larx.” Both were hauled up on
charges of “ insulting behaviour.” Bookie was fined 40s.
and victim 10s. Why the difference, seeing that the
gambling transaction itself was not before the Court, is
only known to minds severely magisterial. Bookie
declared he couldn’t pay the fine. “Then,” said Panton,
“you will go up for 14 days, and it will do you good. ’
We don’t quarrel with the incidence of justice, but it is
not law.
# # #
An artless, but otherwise respectable, citizen
came to sore distress at the North v South Melbourne
football match. In a moment of reckless enthusiasm he
shrieked “ Bully for North !” whereupon the gentleman
standing next to him, a stranger and a free-born ex-
pectorator of blasphemy and tobacco-juice, folded up a
large, bony fist and sent the amateur barracker stealing
silently away with a black eye. That was bully for
Sowth, that was. ,
* # #
Speaking of the “ national game ” of Victoria,
a meeting of Ballarat Wesleyans has passed a resolution
calling upon the leadiug clubs to strike at the “ root of
those evils”—viz., betting and bad language—which are
“ felt to be making it difficult for members of Christian
churches to patronise football matches.” Which strikes
tub Bulletin as being a terribly large Order for the lead-
ing clubs. Besides, why doesn’t the Christian Churqh
itself go and dig in the vineyard for that root, instead of
threatening to stand afar off and barrack for its own
immortal soul ? The duty of reforming football rests with
the Y.M.C.A. who must gather the various secretaries,
goal-sneaks,” captains, managers, and “half backs”
into the fold, and induce them to hurl all bookmakers
(who are puffy and easily hurled) into the outer darkness,
and stick up largo exhortations, painted in white against
a black ground, to swear not at all. Then the “Fitzroy
Forties,” “Collingwood Pusli,” and “ Sons of the Sowth
will flee from the wrath to come, remarking in a pious
pianissimo that “football is dry ’ash,” and the game will
be national no more, Nothing is national unless there s
plenty of cursing in it. Look at the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture, for instance.

play not quite O.K. on one point. They say he pushes
the ball, instead of striking, when he nurses cannons.

V.R.Q. gives Handicapper Barnard £5OO a year
pension, and everyone will allow it is right. Wellington
said: “ The General who never made mistakes never made
war,” and who can remember more than four mistakes by
Barnard?
* * *
Surly, stiff-built old Barnes, the Nottingham
cricketer, is in great form again. Playing against York-
shire he scored an innings of 104, and took four wickets
for 16 runs—a performance which ought to land him in
Lord Sheffield’s team.
# # *
A certain Melbourne “bookie” advertises his
calling by securing, as often as he can, the Governor’s box
in a theatre on big-draw nights, and sits there enthroned
in a blaze of jewellery that would almost crush the Shah
of Persia.
• * *
A typical little history. Rive years ago the
scavengers went out on strike for eight hours per day, and
one of the rank-and-file, who promised well as a nigger-
driver, got a gangership. It had for years been his am-
bition to make a small book, and now came his chance.
It was easy to induce his men to stake a few shillings in his
book. Next we find the ex-scavenger on the Hill, still
doing well. Next year again, in tall hat and broadcloth,
he cries the odds in the Paddock, and at this present
moment he is the proud possessor of some of the very
finest of Australia’s horses and dogs. In the usual course
of things this ex-knight of the broom will be “ doing ”
Europe and giving his opinion on Australian racing.

Harry Stockdale’s Northern Territory buffalo-
shooting party duly left by the Menmuir for Port Essing-
ton. From all we hear, the buffaloes on the mainland
have been pretty' well thinned out of late years, but you
can have some beautiful buffalo—and nigger—shooting on
Melville Island, hard by. The Melville Island niggers
can, in fact, be backed for ferocity against the Brooker
Island people, who ate up Ingham and Co.

* # #
An American account of the Slavin-Kilrain
fight is calculated to inspire a calm Christian peruser to
rush home and strangle his aged parents. “Up they
come for the third round, “Go at him, Jake ! Jump right
into him 1” shouts Cleary, as the men go to the centre of
the ring. Kilrain jumps in with his big arms swinging
like those of a grizzly bear (who ever saw a bear’s arms
swing?). He goes at Slavin full tilt, and there is a dull
rattle of blows which sound like the thudding of flails. It
is awful. Men stand upon their seats, swaying to and fro
with each terrible blow, grunting and groaning, with all
the bloodthirsty instincts of their barbarous forefathers
glowing in unmistakeable characters upon their faces.”
| What sympathy between actors and audience! The
1 patrons of the noble sport grunting and swaying in
unison with the gents who wereflghting for their “purse !”
And then the instincts glowing in characters, like Bel-
shazzar’s writing on the wall—O! it must have been
delicious.
FROM SYDNEY TO MELBOURNE ON
BICYCLES.
INCIDENTS BY THE WAY.
Mr. J. Pearson, of King-street Arcade, the well-
known cycling tourist, when riding over to Mel
bourne with Ins friend, Mr. A. Gr. Chapman,
relates several adventures they met with on their
long journey :
Por four days after leaving Sydney terrible wet
weather was experienced, and many of the water-
courses, which in the summer time are nearly dry,
were swollen so much that they had great diffi-
culty in crossing them.
On one occasion they were compelled to strip
and carry their machines across, the current being
sostrong that they were inimminentdangerof being
swept away. Although they were for several days
riding in wet clothes no dire results accrued, as on
each evening after retiring they took the pre-
caution to rub themselves down with St. Jacobs
Oil, which Mr. Peai'son always carries with him
on his tours, finding it a capital remedy for stiff-
ness and soreness of the muscles, as well as a
preventative and cure for painful ailments
induced by cold.
After getting over the dividing range the
weather changed from wet and cold to excessive
heat. On one day especially the thermometer was
98 in the shade.
Numerous snakes were met with on the journey,
passing several coiled up in the middle of the
road. One monster, over six feet long, caused
them some trouble, as in trying to avoid it the
two cyclists collided, the result of which was a
bruised wrist and a swollen ankle, but the appli-
cation of the ever ready St. Jacobs oil prevented
any bad effects. They reached Melbourne safely,
averaging 64 miles per day, and received a great
reception from their brother wheelmen of the
southern capital.
THE PRINCE OF WALES.
H.R.H. is recognised as being in a bad way by
the Wesleyan Conference sitting in London, or
they would scarcely have considered it necessary
to offer up prayers for his conversion. It is really
not easy to feel anything but sorrow for this genial
but misguided gentleman, who is denounced as a
common spieler, made the subject of ribald ballads
in the democratic press and of cartoons in the
illustrateds. And then, after he has undergone
all these things, and paid the penalty of publicity
in its worst shapes, he is prayed for like a new
recruit at a Salvationist meeting. It scarcely
seems worth while to be a prince now, so great
are the restrictions placed on the occupant of the
billet, although the screw is still good and the
perquisites large and frequent ; but the halcyon
days are gone by for princes and most other
potentates, and in the course of a few fleeting
years they will all be compelled to learn the
unpleasant but inevitable lesson that they can
only be tolerated on condition of being very quiet
and well-behaved, expre sing no opinions and
attracting no attention beyond what is absolutely
necessary when they pay their periodical visits to
the Treasury to draw pension allowances, which
will terminate for ever and ever at their deaths.
Long before that time, however, it will have
become universally recognised that no matter how
many princes there may be, and without any
regard to their morals or peculiarities, there is but
one source of artificial light that can justly and
honestly claim superiority over all others known,
and that is the latest discovery, Luxene, the new
illuminant, about which it is impossible to say
aught but what is good and for the more exact
definition of which there are no expressions in the
English language that can possibly flatter it, or
do it more than common justice. For safety,
economy, brilliancy, and general comfort it has no
compeer and never will have during the lifetime
of this generation. Luxene is a staple of supply
with Messrs. John Barlow and Co. ; T. W. Eady
and Son ; Mason Bros., Limited ; D. Mitchell
and Co. ; Tilloek and Co. : J. Keep and Sons;
Holdsworth, MTherson, and Co.; M‘Lean Bros,
and Rigg, Limited ; of Fred. Butcher, and Robt.
Miller, Sydney ; J. Ireland, R. Hall and Sons,
and David Miller, Newcastle.
ONE MAN ONE VOTE.
ONE DAY ONE ELECTION.
ONE PEOPLE ONE DESTINY.
That is what the present eruption in the political
world means, if it means anything; and if the
people’s representatives who have headed the polls
in those electorates which have been hitherto kept
sacred to the brazen-faced god of property can
keep their heads, and go slow, and maintain their
integrity in spite of all the machinery that will be
set in motion to circumvent them, and resist to
the death every attempt made to convert them
intoCs.M.G. and lords and “docks” and other
similarly idle and frequently vagrant personages.
In which case the Destiny is a foregone conclusion
for which it is not at present necessary to find a
name, and the People will be the most prosperous
and Australia the nearest approach to a model
country this side of the millennium. To help on
this most desirable prospect it will be necessary
to have also
ONE TIME AND ONE TIMEPIECE,
the latter being of necessity the Waterbary
Watch, which is the only timekeeper that can bo
relied on to do everything that a .good, watch
should do, and never to go back on its principles
or its owner.

POLITICAL POINTS
Labour-politics are not party-politics.
N.S, Wales needs more national politics,
and less metropolitics.
Toby Barton “ does not admit that politics
is a profession.” It’s all profession.
Electors of Australia, fight'for the key of
the front-door of your inheritance : One Man One
Vote.
Teetotaller Munro, on the beer-tax, shows
that he can swallow principles even if he can’t
swallow beer.
Until One-Man-One-Vote becomes a fact,
the people will only “stand about the portals
of Government.”
“Governments have always discouraged
independent and original talent, and have always
promoted mediocrity. ” —Gorst,
Barton used 280 I’s to Jack Want’s 200 in
his N.S.W. Assembly speech on the Imperialisa-
tion of Australia Inoculation Bill.
Latest from Maoriland :—That in future
the Upper Chamber be made elective and that
a 1 members be elected exclusively by the female
fraichise.
Powers, M.P., moves in Queensland
Assembly for one-man-one-vote, all elections on
cne day, and the closing ol public-houses on
election-day.
Munro’s talk of economy in the Vic-
torian Civil Service has resulted in a proposed
saving of £5680 for the coming year. What is
that among so many ?
Dr. Cockburn (S.A.), who moves for the
abolition of party-Government, has submitted,
amid “cheers and laughter,” a resolution for the
election of Ministers by the House.
Premier Munro: “I believe in getting
money when we can.” How Scotch ! Now for
the honest dishonest politician who will say, “I
believe in getting money how we can.”
The latest is that ex-Treasurer Garvan
have been dining with Premier Parkes. Here
you may perhaps see why the old man is keeping
the door of the N.S.W. Treasury open.
A “formal motion for adjournment ” in
the Queensland Parliament, the other day, lasted
the whole of the sitting. It was an attack on
railway mismanagement by E. J. Stevens, and the
motion was only withdrawn 5 minutes before
midnight. This is almost unique in parliamentary
procedure.
During 1890-1, N.S.W. exported one-third
of the total Australian and N.Z. yield of wool.
Her wool-sales were 251,314 bales. Her total im-
ports, £22,615,004; exports, £22,045,973.
Jawbone Neild is the hero of the shortest
as well as of the longest speech in the N.S.W
Assembly. His speech on the recent no-confidence
motion is represented by six lines of Hansard.
Parkes one day denies that he knows any-
thing of Mr. Cotton. Yet next day, almost, he
says Mr. Cotton “would grace any deliberative
assembly in the world or sometlfng like that.
Mystery.
Premier Munro thinks that by auctioning
some public lands and issuing £750,000 Treasury
bonds Victoria can, with economy and care, get
through the year very nicely without additional
taxation.
The cause of Independent Australia lias
many advocates in the N.S.W Parliament.
Miller, from Monaro, spoke warmly in its favour
the other night, to a sympathetic, if sleepy-looking,
Assembly.
What price now for Canadian public and
political morality as a pleasant foil to the
“Republican corruption” of the United States,
so continuously referred to in the English and
Australian daily press ?
Australia leads, England follows. .“One-
man-one-vote ” will be a feature of the next
English general election. The doughty old
champion, Sir George Grey, will, in all proba-
bility, be there to support it.
“ I simply give my word against that of
the hon. gentleman (Nick Willis), knowing that
those who know me will take my word, and that
those who do not know the hon. gentleman may
believe him.” —Frank Cotton.
Minister of Railways Unmack declares
that the Queensland Railway Commissioners have
already saved their salaries several times over.
Shiels says the Victorian Commissioners have cost
the Southern colony several millions extra.
George Reid appears to be genuinely
delighted at the entrance of the N.S.W. Labour-
party into Parliament, which is, he says, speaking
as one who wishes to see work done, tlie healthiest
and most hopeful sign he has seen for years.
“Tom” Rose, M.L.A., say’s the N.S.W.
Labour-party are “only Freetraders in disguis o ,”
Had they voted to put Rose’s party into office
John Haynes would have styled them “veiled
Protectionists.” You can’t please everybody.
“Hi never copy Victoria.” Yet, in the
very next breath, Parkes announced his intention
of introducing a measure for the self-registration of
electors —a plan which a quarter of a century a; °
emanated from the brain of R. D. Ireland, Q.O.
In N.S. W, Assembly. Shearer Williams
speaks:-“I believe in Federation” (Parkes :
“ ’Ear, ’ear ”), “ but I am totally opposed to the
proposed Constitution ” (the House; “ Hear,
hear”), and, what is more, 1 will oppose it.”
(Parkes exit.)
Every Australian Bishop consecrated in
England, literally holds his See and all its spirit-
ualities and temporalities on condition of being
faithful to Queen Victoria and her heirs. Which
is another strong argument for consecrating the
Australian chief-shepherds here.
“ Hon. members have declared that a
knowledge of English literature and English law
is not sufficient to enable a man to enter the legal
profession. Why, these two subjects comprise all
the knowledge on the face of the earth, literary
and legal,”— Sir George Grey.
Toby Barton (18 months ago) at Gran-
ville : —“Protection will come years before Federa-
tion ;of that I am sure. One misconception which
has arisen is that I am going to sink the fiscal
controversy until Federation is attained. No
more nonsensical misconception ever existed in
the world.”
On Crispi’s motion the Italian Parliament
has unanimously voted a Mazzini monument.
The King subscribes largely, but this reminds you
of Louis Philippe’s fatal mistake in fetching Napo-
leon’s remains from St. Helena, whence Thiers’
revival of the Napoleonic Legend in his great
history.
The N.S.W. Labour-elected members who
want to summarily throw out Parkes, instead of
making him their obedient servant against his
own will, need a liberal education in the art of
revenge. Their present ideas on the subject are
those current in Tipperary and on the Svdney
“Rocks.”
Tom Glassey, the Queensland member who
could only get three supporters in Parliament the
other day, will yet lead as big and as compact a
following as did Parnell in the strongest time of
the Home Rule party. The mere fact that he
made Chief Secretary Tozer furiously savage
shows that his tactics are telling.
