To the Individualist liberty means a man’s
freedom to do as he likes, trammelled only by the
possession of similar rights by others. To the
Socialist liberty means a man’s freedom to do as
he ought; a making his duty to society the chief
corollary of the opportunity for action arising
from the recognition of his right to freedom.
The fact that Victoria’s customs’ revenue
for 1890-1 was less than anticipated, only proves
that Victoria now grows or makes the
articles she was formerly compelled to import.
Analysis of the returns bears this out. Foreign-
grown or manufactured articles show decreases;
but exotics such as tea, coffee and chocolate show
increases.
Writes David Taller man :
Ireland suffers because it has excluded the Jewish
element from its people ; for the commercial intelligence
of our race would not have allowed the natural advantages
that Ireland possesses to lie dormant. No people could
compete with Ireland in the supply to the English markets
if matters were properly managed.
“Why is Ireland poor and discontented ?” Be-
cause she has no Jews !
Parkes’ first Government business on the
official paper, be it noted, was not One-man-one-
vote, but a motion affirming the beauty of woman-
suffrage. He deliberately gave precedence to this
fad, and thus brought the business paper into con-
flict with the Governor’s speech. He jibs on fully
enfranchising the men, and shows symptoms of
being under petticoat government.
M‘Millan’s administration of the N.S.W.
Treasury and Railways, summed up by Schey,
parliamentary mouthpiece of the railway and
tramway employes:—“Mr. M'Millan, the ac-
countant, uses figures to prove facts ; Mr. M‘M.,
the politician, uses words to distort facts. For
Mr. M'M., the accountant, I have a profound
respect; for Mr. M'M., the politician, I have
nothing but the most profound contempt.”
The Bulletin notices the formation in
one of the Sydney suburbs of a “ Freetrade Labour
E'ect oral League.” The Labour elector who joins
and at the poll adheres to either a Freetrade or
Protectionist organisation is simply sawing at
Labour’s carotid artery. Labour’s only chance
is solidarity consequent on the litter sinking of
the fiscal question. The Freetrade Labour Elec-
toral League promoters are wool-clad wolves.
Sydney Echo declares against One-Man-
One-Vote, which, it says, “is against the best
sense of the country.” What Sydney Echo says,
the S.M. Herald thinks, but suppresses ; the
other alternative is that the S.M. Herald pro-
prietary are guilty of the immorality of vending
for money two classes of political opinions. There
is a desperate fight ahead, dear friends, over One-
Man-One-Vote, which is the foundation of ail
things.
The Bulletin doesn’t care a rap what
“ decision ” was given 40 years ago in re the
precedence of ecclesiastical dignitaries in Aus-
tralia. All “decisions” on the subject, not
founded on the bedrock fact that there is no
State Church in Australia, and that in the eye
of the Australian law the Particular Baptists are
just as big as the whole Roman Church, with the
Pope thrown in, are mere flam and waste-paper.
All are equal, because in the eye of the State none
are anything.
President Storey, of Sydney Chamber of
Commerce, speaking of the great strike said :
“Now that the labour-struggle is over who can
say that good will no'; result?” Now that ivhat
is over? “The trial of strength?” Bah! It is
one continuous, never ending, never slacken-
ing trial. It is the bioplasm of the centuries;
the warfare of the oppressed against the
oppressors; the inevitable corollary of human
wrongs, and it must endure so long as evil lives
upon the earth. Over ?
Say what men may, the argument ad-
vanced that the sexes are mentally equal is
nonsense. The strong woman in the circus may
be stronger than any man present, but the average
of the men who look on at her are far stronger
than the average of the women. And as it is
physically, so, in all human probability, it is men-
tally, not only as regards developed actualities,
but also in respect to latent possibilities. That
is not the ground on which woman may fairly seek
the suffrage.
The storm in the A.N.A. teapot about a
certain Major Hoad has ended in Alfred Deakin
and the “ natives ” generally being satisfied that
the Major’s retirement from active service in the
Association was necessary to the proper fulfilment
of his military duties. Thank goodness that’s all
right. Why the A.N.A. should ever have had
such an insane desire to hang on to a Majah is a
problem that no republican new-chum can solve.
In fact, the purpose and policy of the A.N.A.
becomes more hopelessly muddled and mixed
every week.
The N.S.W. Labour-party voted against
Parkes’ abstract motion concerning woman
suffrage, simply because they did not wish the
Electoral Reform Bill delayed or thrown out.
For similar reasons they voted against Fitzgerald’s
ill-advised amendment, after it had been accepted
as a vote of censure by the Premier. The U pper
House want an excuse to delay One-man-one-vote,
a measure for which the province has unanimously
declared; Parkes, with his womanhood suffrage
(on which the country has not spoken), was about
to furnish them with one.
Parkes’ Electoral Bill should be amended
so as to ’prevent any Premier or Government in
the future from using the machinery of Govern-
ment to defeat Opposition candidates in the
various constituencies. The Bulletin rises to
suggest that it should be made an offence, punish-
able by a heavy fine, for a Minister of the Crown
to hire a special tram, to offer a bridge, a road, a
railway, or a courthouse in return for minis-
terial support, to “J.P.” or offer to “J.P.”
political wire-pullers, to promise to “M.L.C.” a
wavering candidate if he happens to be defeated,
or in any other way to interfere with the free
action of a constituency.
The exclusion at will of strangers from
Parliament is an antiquated relic of tyrannical
times. The people—so long as they behave them-
selves (and the House has plenary powers of
punishment for misbehaviour within its pre-
cincts) —should be free to attend all sessions of
the People’s House. Secret sittings are inherently
bad in a deliberative Assembly supposed to bo
responsible to the electors. To say that men can
send otheis into a paid Parliament, and must not
be permitted to watch how the latter act when
they get there, is to state a lopsided proposition
equivalent to arguing that an employer should be
debarred from observing his employees at work.
The present corrupt J.P. system won’t be
remedied by the appointment of proletarian
magistrates. The honorary J.P. is no good any-
how. He knows no law, but thinks he knows
everything. Amateur J.P.’s should everywhere,
and at whatever'cost, be superseded by professional
magistrates, and the latter, instead of being
allowed to settle down in one place and grow grey-
headed among vested interests, should be shifted
every three years and absolutely prohibited from
belonging to any secret society. During the last
20 years, wherever we have been in Australia, we
have found Sullivan, J.P., hearing the case of
his cousin Ryan, J.P., and Jones rounding up
friendly beaks to take the starch out of his
ancient enemy, Jones.
Dear Bulletin, —J. J, C. Abbott, Q.C.,
leader of the Montreal bar, highly paid solicitor
for the Canadian Pacific Railway, recently chosen
by the Tory party of Canada to carry out the
Imperialistic policy of Sir John Macdonald, held
different political views in his younger days.
Way back in the fifties he was one of the signers
of the Annexation Manifesto, a sort of political
round-robin in which the signers, after naming
various reasons for the faith that was in them,
pledged themselves to agitate for the union of the
Canadian provinces with the United States.
Copies of this interesting document are still
extant, though most of the signers are in their
graves, and a majority of the remainder dislike
any reference to the pamphlet. By the way, what
became of Abbott’s much-advertised visit to Aus-
tralia “to promote commercial intercourse with
Canada ?” He got as far as London on his way to
the Antipodes, some 12 months ago. and then
apparently turned tail. Did the Agcnts-General
throw cold water on his mission ? Kanuck.

The institution of monarchism must of its
own weakness die. Monarchism is an unhealthy
loaferdom, and anything that has laziness is con-
sequently weak, mentally and physically. Unless
the Guelphs and the Hohenzollerns and all the
other cancerous and gouty kings and queens of
the Royal houses of Europe whose blood has been
filtered through centuries of vice and laziness and
languor decide to be treated at the polypotent
Bethel Medical Baths, Lonsdale-street, Mel-
bourne, they are doomed as a family. As the
representatives of the fatuous idiocy of bygone
years which-has deified them as possessing a right
to govern the people, never given by the people,
they are dying rapidly, and in order for them or
any gouty or neuralg c martyr like them to save
their bodies they must adopt the course advocated
in this par.*
Avery simple cure for rheumatism has
been unearthed by a correspondent, who writes
on the subject in the following unique style (we
give his epistle verbatim ct httcratim) : —“ Dear
Sir, —I ain’t much ova ritur, but 1 think as how
it’s my dooty to let yer no wot a good menny don’t
no—and that is how to knar rumatics. I suffers
from this komplant fearful, at Rest I did, and mi
pore legs gets all kramped up with it on nites.
In fakt my ole wooman she ses that she leant go
on like this and hev me disturbin of her and if I
kant do nothin for it she’d hev to get a bed for
hersel. Thet wasn’t kind you’ll admit, but it set
me thinkin and talkin, I was advized ter try hot
salt-water baths reglar. That’s simple nuf ses I,
but where to get the baths mi fiend. Oh ses he,
no trubble abowt thet, go to the Natertoorum
[Natatorium, we presume.— Ed. B.] in Pitt-street;
and you ken always hev wun there, well wud yer
bleeve me Sur, 1 wont there every day fur neerly
three weekes, and I’m blesst if the paine ain’t all
gone. I rekkon its all along ov the hot baths (sea-
water) there, which you can get at all hours. The
waiter’s alius warm and plessent, and by bavin a
cold shour after there’s no danger ov cold. At
leest that’s what I’ve found, and 1 thort I’d let
you know. Yer can make wot use yer like ov
this.”*
The Death of Strikes —the Federation of Labour

“HE'D RATHER GO TO HELL.”
“ Rather than see Australia a Republic he would pray
God Almighty to send him to the nethermost hell.’’—
Squatter Armytage, M.L.A. for Grant, in the Victorian
Assembly, during Federation Bill debate.
Victoria ! how thy aged heart
Will thrill with fervent pleasure
When cablegrams to thee impart
The secret of a—treasure—
A subject, who, for love of thee,
Would gaily to Inferno “ gee.”
Yes, sooner than this Austral land
Should snap the “ silken fetters,”
And ’mongst the nations take her stand
Proclaiming none her betters,
This loyal youth, who loves thee well,
Would lake a berth in deepest hell !
Bright hopes illume the squatter’s breast
And fond anticipation
Of Royal visits to his “ nest ”
To follow Federation.
And shall foul knaves his dreams dispel?
No ! No ! he’d rather go to hell!
W.J.H.

An English Army officer proposes to wipe
out desertion and renewed enlistment, by tatooing
all recruits.
The Central Gold-mining Cos., of Perth,
W.A., the first of the dividend-paying companies
at the new Yilgarn fields, is now giving a regular

Mr. Miller, the manager of the Bank of
New South Wales, was at latest under a course of
massage treatment, at Victor Horsley’s private
hospital, in London, and there is every prospect
of his perfect recovery.
Many of the temperance-drinks which do
not contain over one per cent, of alcohol, are, so
says that great chemist, F. C. Robinson, more
dangerous than raw whisky. They contain
salicylic acid, used to prevent fermentation, but
which brings on Bright’s disease.

A few years ago whilst an escort was
bringing down a string of Chinese prisoners to
Rockhampton, (Q.) gaol two of them escaped.
To save bother at headquarters and keep tally the
troopers skirmished around and arrested the first
two Mongolians—all Mongols look alike to the
unpractised white—they came across. These
happened to be a couple of pensive fossickers.
Nobody knew the difference except the two
unfortunates and it didn’t matter anyhow. They
c ouldn’t vote and they don’t buy newspapers.

Mr. William Smyth, M.L.A.
SENIOR MEMBER FOR GYMPIE QUEENSLAND.
Mb, William Smyth, whose portrait we give in
this notice, is undoubtedly one of the most popular
men on the Gympie goldfields, as also one of the
largest holders in some of the best-paying gold-
mines of Queensland. He was born in Sydney in
1845, and received his education at the school of
the Rev. Mr. Kemmiss, Macquarie-street, the
Rev. A. Boag, of Princess and afterwards Liver-
pool-street, and finally at the Fort-street National
School. Being of a roving and adventurous dis-
position, he chose gold-mining as his avocation,
and at the age of 16 years worked on the Jembai-
cumbene in the Bcaidwood district, and after-
wards at Araluen On the latter field he was
engaged in constructing machinery for raising
water and treating the auriferous earth. On the
decline of that field Mr. Smyth proceeded to
Forbes, and from thence to Grenfell where he
worked with a fair amount of success. The rich
finds at Gympie attracted a number of miners
from the other colonies, amongst others the subject
of our sketch, who arrived in Gympie ip March,
1868. Since then he has experienced the usual
ups and downs incidental to mining life. For
many years he ably managed several of the
principal mines on that field, his thorough
practical knowledge of gold-mining in all
its branches giving him an advantage such
as only a few men possess. As usual with miners,
his surplus earnings went to develop some of the
mines, and for eleven years without success. He,
however, had been steadily paying into the No. 1
North Phoenix, and when payable gold was struck
in this mine Mr. Smyth’s fortune was made. No.
1 North Phoenix is one of the most consistent
dividend-paying gold-mines in Queensland, the
total amount paid in dividends up to date being
£377,500. Mr. Smyth has ably filled the office of
Mayor of the town, having been elected during his
absence from the field. He was elected some
years ago as senior Member for Gympie in the
Liberal interest, and since then has done yeoman
service in representing the wants and requirements
of the electorates. Mr. Smyth is a great enthusi-
ast in the matter of athletic sports in which at
one time he took an active part, he is, further,
exceedingly popular among all native-born Aus-
tralians, and was recently unanimously elected
President of the Australian Natives’ Association.
His brother, Mr. J. J. Smyth has long been iden-
tified with Gunnedah, N.S.W., and has filled the
office of "Mayor of Gunnedah for five years in
succession.

Darwin) says it is a mistake to prosecute Chinese

It is noted that as the alligators in Cairns
Inlet, N.Q.. thin out, the sharks increase visibly.
It is stated that a shark always takes his wife
with him when hunting alligators, and the pair
generally manage to polish off one erocky between
them.
In Perth (W. A.) Supreme Court the other
day, Barrister Moorhead insinuated that Barrister
Hensman was usually “connected with dirty
cases.” Hensman protested and appealed to
Judge Stone, who turned abruptly to the jury
and said —“ Well, gentlemen of the jury, Mr.
Hensman is sufficiently long here for you to know
him better than I do. ”
Weerite (Vic.) recently agitated for a
school by petition, which was forwarded to the
Education office by farmer Bradley. Chief-clerk
Handheld replied thereto, and District-Inspector
Roche was sent up to report. Ere he could do so
he was transferred elsewhere, and Inspector Car-
michael was sent in his stead. All this within
two or three months. Bradley, Handheld, Roche
and Carmichael have all four died during the last
month, and the Weeriteans are asking to be made
the subjects of special prayer.

What a ghastly display of man-doing machinery !
The National Advocate (Bathurst,
N.S.W.) is querulous as to “The Bulletin’s
consistency.” The Bulletin is prepared toargue
the question of its consistency with any man on
earth—except the one man who, after publicly
cursing drink as the devil’s brew that fills The Pit
with lost and wallowing victims, accepted for a
price the secretaryship to the Sydney Bung
Association, and, in that capacity, anathematised
just as liberally and luridly at teetotal fanaticism,
and helped on the glorious cause of drunkenness
and general hell-fire with a zeal exactly propor-
tionate to the dollars paid him. It would be
folly to argue about consistency with a Dissolving
View; the remarks would become too personal.
North and South Queensland appear to be
gradually squeezing out Central Queensland, and
if South Australia reaches out (by extending rail-
way communication from Hergott Springs Junc-
tion to Teeta) for the far western trade, Rock-
hampton will dwindle as a port of irnmediatecom-
mercial importance. Some of the trade that
formerly found exit at Rockhaihpton has already
gone—southwards to Brisbane via Charleville,
and northwards to Townsville, via Hughenden.
Should another stream flow westwards and south-
wards to Hergott Springs and Adelaide, Rock-
hampton will have to wait until the trade of its
own immediate country becomes big enough to
fill its hungry bingie. Central Queensland Separa-
tionigts had better be judiciously diplomatic with
'Premier Griffith in the matter of his scheme of
tripartite autonomous provinces.
George Reid points out that the Broken
Hill Proprietary Mine, Worth ten minions of
pounds sterling to-day and yielding last year one
million pounds, actually pays to the State only
£3O in annual rent. Already in N.S. W, GOO men;
have millions of acres more than the whole
ing 34,000 free-holders. Municipal interests are
confined to but 2000 square miles of territory.
Over 318,000 square miles, Government has
squandered millions of money on behalf of those
who never contribute a farthing a year. Within
62,000 square miles struggling freeholders struggle
hard to keep roofs over their heads, contributing
for every yard of gutter and street in their neigh-
bourhood, while over 308,000 square miles one sees
vast estates, owned by wealthy absentees, sur-
rounded by spacious roads paid for by the masses
of the people, without a single sixpence being con-
tributed in return.
There is a great art in knowing just how
to go bung—to pay your creditors little or nothing
and yet'rake in your own dues. In the Far North
Dobbs keeps a store for Nobbs, but in Dobbs’
own name—say, on a diggings. Nobbs runs
another in some town or city. Dobbs and Nobbs
file their schedules, separately and jointly. Forty
others of their own kidney—all business men with
a leaning towards insolvency—call a meeting and
weep over the firm’s misfortune; big-headed,
windy men get up and say how much good Dobbs
has done for the district, and thunder out curses
on the diggers—customers —because they don’t
get more gold out of the ground and pay Dobbs’
and Nobbs’accounts 17 times over. Also Nobbs
has come-up from town to wind up the firm’s
affairs, and he reads out a list of names that no
one ever heard of before —all good marks, blefs
you ! These book-debts are obviously worth sell-
ing, and Jobbs, confidential-clerk to Nobbs, buys
the same. Dobbs pays in the £, and is feted
by the weeping 40 sympathisers, who let him go
at that. Nobbs goes back to town and gets feted
by blubberers there. Jobbs starts to prosecute
everybody who does owe the firm, and crosses out
of his list the strange names that puzzled the 40
conspirators. Jobbs levies on tents, properties,
leases, and generally cleans out the field, and goes
back to be confidential clerk to Nobbs. Dobbs
starts afresh to do more good for the district.
In reference to enquiries made by The
Bulletin concerning the so-called biography of
Bradlaugh, extracts from which lately appeared in
a Sydney Sunday paper, Mrs. Bradlaugh-Bonner,
writing from London under date 12th June,
says :
Dear Bulletin, —The so-called biography from which
Sydney Truth (!) condescends to publish extracts is a
malicious libel from beginning to end. The alleged pub-
lisher (Gunn) was legally declared non-existent, the'
alleged author (Mackayj went bankrupt, and Mr. Brad-
langh commenced a suit against the really responsible
person (W. Stewart Ross). Just before the case came on
for trial Mr. Ross went to Mr. Bradlaugh s solicitors to
beg that the matter should not be proceeded with. Mr.
Bradlaugh kindly consented to stay proceedings on con-
dition of the legal order of which a copy is subjoined
being made. 1 have reason to believe that an effort is
being made to push the circulation of this libel now that
Mr. Bradlaugh is dead—such is the courage of hisenemies!
—but I have taken legal advice, and, if necessary, I shall
institute proceedings myself, for the manly libellers
thought it consistent with their exalted notions of honour
to vilify even the daughters of the man they hated. lam
writing a short note to the editor of Truth, and I have no
doubt that, if he be an honourable gentleman, he will
apologise for the wanton slander that I really cannot but
think he has inadvertently published. Thanking you for
your letter,— l am, yours sincerely, J. Bradlaugii-Boxner.
Copy -of the legal order made in reference to the
libellous “Life of Charles Bradlaugh,” alleged to have
been written by Chas. H. Mnckay :
“ In the High Court of Justice, Queen’s Bench Division,
1888, 8., 573. Master in Chambers, the Hon. Robert
Butler. Between Charles Bradlaugh, plaintiff, and Wm,
Stewart Ross, Charles U. Madiuy, and-L. J, Gunn,
defendants.
“Upon hearing the solicitors for the plaintiff and
defendant, William Stewart Ross, it is ordered that upon
the defendant, William Stewart Ross, undertaking to
account to the satisfaction of the plaintiff’s solicitors for,
and destroy within four days, all copies of the book
entitled, ‘The Life of Charles Bradlaugh, M.P.,’ the
above-named plaintiff, containing the alleged libels, tho
subject matter of this action, which have come into his
possession or control; and upon payment in the name of
the plaintiff of the sum of £5O to the treasurer of the
Masonic Boys’ School, at Freemasons’ Tavern, Great Queen-
street, Lincoln’s Inn Fields, and the plaintiff’s costs of
this action as against all parties to be taxed as between
solicitor and client, this action be discontinued, and all
further proceedings with regard to the said libels be
stayed, and (on the above terms being carried out) that
the record be withdrawn. It is ordered that the money
deposited for security for costs account by each party be
paid out to the plaintiff on account of the above costs.
Dated tho 15th day of February, 1889.
“P.S.—I may add further that suits were commenced
in respect of the publication of this libel against the Dum-
fries Standard, which paid £lO to the Masonic Boys’
School, made an apology, and paid the costs ; the Scotch
printers of the libel, who paid £25 to the school, made an
apology, and paid the costs ; and the Warrington
Observer, which also paid £25 to the school, expressed
regret, and paid the costs.—J. B. B.”

The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson,
lies before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while transferable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away or
given as security, while bearing interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 6 months; 6
par cent. ; 3 months, 5 per cent. But special rates
can be procured for longer or shorter periods.
The Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years, 9i
per cent. ;12 months, per cent. ; 6 months, 7|
per cent. ; 3 months, per cent., and during the
last four years a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
to all depositors in addition to the current rates
on their deposits. This is the only Bank in Aus-
ralasia that thus divides its profits.*

A Uor anchor in the broad, smooth sheet of

from Balaclava, crammed in every hole and

raiments, including as many horses as there
was room for, going into winter quarters near
Hit ouaint little Turkish village of the same name
the Gulf The Commissariat and forage had
■ilreadv arrived from the Golden Horn in a smart-
Wikintr barque called the Bomarsund, discharged
a fortnight before, and lying there wind-bound
with 20 straight miles of narrow water between
her and the Marmora, with a due west course to
make, and a steady nine-knot westerly breeze
blowing that never let up night or day for half-
an-hour at a time.
Soldiers and horses were all safely landed
from the Himalaya, a general clean-up was in
urogress under the watchful eye of Rich, the first
lieutenant, and steam was ordered for daylight
the next morning. A dingy pulled by two men
came alongside from the, Bomarsund, and
squat red-headed, and miquitously-freckled
man climbed up out of the stern - sheets
and came on board. Benjamin Fentland
Priest Commander, R.N., and* captain
of H.M.S. Himalaya, was taking his after-
breakfast walk on the quarter-deck, and reached
the turn near the gangway just as the stranger
arrived, holding in his hand a somewhat dirty
and crumpled-looking letter. The ship’s corporal
was about to interpose, when the freckled man,
with a confidential wave of a massive paw, joy-
fully observed : “ Dom it, mon, I’m in luck after
a’ They tell’t me ashore yonder that ye’d maist
like be awa’ shootin’ ducks. I’m thinkin’ ye’re
the, skipper o’ this craft though, eh?”
44 We11, sir?”
“Weel sir! Ou aye, I understand, ye ken.
I’m a skipper raysel’, so you needna be sae stand-
offish. Yon’s ma ship, and here’s a letter tae
VG.
Commander Priest took the unsavoury-looking
document, opened it and read, and as he read he
scowled; then tore it into small fragments, and
pitched them over the side.
44 That’s my answer,” he remarked, and resumed
his walk aft. .
44 Eli! That’s your answer, is it: But, dom
it, it’ll no do me. Are ye gaun’ to tow me out o’
this blavvsted gulf, or are ye no? That’s what I
want to ken, and gin ye pit me on ony o’ your
blawsted airs wi’ me, my mon, 111 just gang ashore
to the Commeesary yonder, and see if he’ll no
male’ ye- do it!” .
By this time Captain Priest had again reached
the gangway “ Silence, sir, and check your
insolent tongue Do you see that flag, and the
pennant over your head ? Her Majesty’s ships are
not at the disposal of any jackanapes of a soldier,
no matter what his rank may be. Leave the ship,
sir. Into your boat, and as quickly as may be.”
“Her Majesty’s ship, indeed. What ah——
o’ a lang tail oor cat’s got! So ye’ll no chuck me
a tow-line, eh? Why, dom it, mon, I’m on Her
Mawjesty’s service as weel as yoursel’. Didna’ I
bring the Commeesary man and the stores doun
to this h fired hole on a contrac’? I was
minded to ask you on to ma ship to dinner the
nicht, but noo I’ll see ye d d first, an’ I’ll jist
gang to the Commeesary and see if he canna’
teach ye ceveelity !”
“ Youngster, tell the master-at-arms to put this
man over the side, and if he doesn’t go quietly to
throw him over !” Saying which, the master of
the situation turned on his heel and went below.
“Dom yer maister-o’-airms and you, too, ye
And just then he was peremptorily helped into
his boat, but the stream of his eloquence was un-
impeded as long as he was within range.
An hour afterwards came a quartermaster with
“ The captain wants you on deck, sir.”
“ Youngster, take the cutter and go on board
the Bomarsund, and find out if there are any men
on board who will volunteer for naval service.”
“ Am I to ask her captain’s permission, sir?”
“ No. Carry out your instructions. Take the
coxswain of the boat on deck with you and turn
the hands up yourself. If there is any inter-
ference return and report.”
The Himalaya was short-handed by about 20
men, some of whom had shortly before been
transferred to the Terrible, while a few had broken
their leave and been left behind at Constan-
tinople.
At the end of about three-quarters of an hour
the cutter returned, and the youngster reported :
“ Boarded the Bomarsund, sir, the captain came
forrard as I was speaking to the bo’sun and
ordered me into the boat—said he’d chuck me oyer
and swamp the boat if I didn’t go at once. Tried
to remonstrate with him, but he refused to
listen.”
“Very good; go back again. Take a file of
marines with you and handcuffs. Carry out your
instructions, and if he again interferes make him
prisoner and bring him here.”
Another hour passed before the cutter returned,
bringing with her on this occasion the wretched
freckled man, held down by the marines in the
bottom of the boat. The youngster reported
“Turned up the hands on the Bomarsund, sir,
nine men volunteer out of sixteen ; say their wages
are overdue and they can get nothing out of the
captain but promises. He went to his cabin for a
rifle and threatened to fire. Made him prisoner,
and he is now in the cutter alongside.”
“Very good; send him aft here. Take the
port life-boat and go back to the Bomarsund and
bring the volunteers and their bags on board.”
And then followed one of the queerest scenes
ever enacted on the deck of one of Her Majesty's
ships. As the freckled red man came aft between
the two marines, and got his eye on his enemy the
fountain of his rage was broken up into separate
torrents of blasphemy and withering denunciation
flowing with a volubility that was in no degree
impaired by the northern burr that emphasized
them. Neither threats nor appeals to his reason
were of any service, and his wind never failed him
for a second. Nobody escaped his notice, and
nothing between Heaven and Sheol got left out,
and still he wouldn’t stop, but just struggled, and
bit, and kicked, until he was sent below, where
he finally was put in irons and gagged. At the
end of an hour he was all right again, signed
an order on his Constantinople agents for the
men’s wages—which was duly honoured ' on pre-
sentation-kicked over a tray on which a steward
handed him a tumbler and the needful, rushed
down the ladder into the ship’s dingy offered for
his return, and with one last blood-curdling curse
for the Himalaya from truck to kelson, and
everybody in her, finishing with an awful swear
to destroy yon “ commeesary ” man, root and
branch for having made “ sic-like a d——d fule o’
him,” we parted for ever. The Himalaya steamed
westward at sunrise the following morning, leaving
the Bomarsund to wait for a change of wind and
another crew. Whether his curses came home to
roost, who can tell? Up to date the old ship
doesn’t look any the worse for them.
V. V.
The Bulletin’s London correspondent
says that the Savage Club, having emerged
triumphantly from its monetary troubles, is now,
under Sir Somers Vine, about to cast off the last
shred of Bohemianism. No more frayed linen is
to be seen in the Club, and several poor and lowly
members have been sacrificed at the shrine of
Respectability up to date. A new departure has
just been made‘in the shape of “ ladies’nights,”
after the manner of the Lyric and Meistersingers.
The first essay was a success, and the innovation
will be popular with a certain section of the
members. The “old hands,” however, shake
their heads, and begin to regret the ancient days
in the Savoy and elsewhere, ere the Savages
became So intensely respectable.
W. S. Gilbert now sits as an honorary
magistrate on the Middlesex Bench.—
The impoverished thief
Who has stolen some beef,
Or the boy who has snavelled a filbert,
Must twang a guitar
When he stands at the bar
To be tried by the humorous Gilbert,
And plead for his pity
By singing a ditty—
Perhaps, though, a pun would be properer
(The song, or the pun,
Being certainly one
From a Gilbert and Sullivan opera).
Then the author would say—
“ Little nut, run away
And buy a lot more with this money,”
And would offer relief
To a filcher of beef
Who was doing his best to be funny.
For, though the sharp claw
Of a merciless law
Must seize on the people who purloin,
Sure, a quip or a song
Can atone for what’s wrong
In the theft of a filbert or sirloin.
O ! picture the joy
Of the man and the boy—
Both indulging in jubilant dances,
And shouting “ Hip ! hip !”
When permitted to skip
On the magistrate's recognizances.
The “ peelers ” and “traps ”
Would be swearing,,perhaps,
That they never had heard such a din afore,
And want it subdued,
But the show would conclude
With the “Englishman’s” chorus from “Pinafore.,”

reports : “ There are over 7,000,000 young men in
America to-day, of whom over 5,000,000 never
darkened a church door. Seventy-five out of
every hundred of these j'oung men do not attend
church ; ninety-five out of every hundred do not
belong to church, and ninety-seven out of every
hundred do nothing to spread Christianity.”
And, he might have added, out of the five who do
go to church, two or thereabouts go to gaol for
mean and hypocritical offences.
The late Edwin Long’s “A Question of
Propriety” is about the most valuable, and cer-
tainly the best-bought, picture in the Melbourne
Gallery. It is the original, snapped off the easel'
for £300 before he painted a larger edition of the
same subject for the Reyal Academy show. A
wonderful bargain this, as compared with the
“ Esther ” replica, which hangs on the opposite
wall and cost, in a then rising market, £1600.
Long’s pictures have fetched wonderful prices, at
times. Holloway, of pill celebrity, paid £8000 for
“The Babylonian Marriage Market ” to adorn his
noble sanatorium for lunatics, although the
wisdom of selecting such a spicy subject to interest
minds diseased was rather doubtful. Of late the
critics have been complaining that Long was too
fond of painting naked young ladies and palming;
them off as lofty Scriptural conceptions.
“Tommy” has a selection six miles from
a rising town on the River Darling. Whenever
“ Ted,” who lives further afield, makes for the
-town, he invariably calls at Tommy’s to break the
journey, generally arriving at about 2 o’clock in
the morning, when his habit is to quietly disrobe
and climb over the sleeping Tommy and bunk
“ inside ” him without ceremony. Now, recently,
another friend of Tommy wived, and Tommy lent
his selection to the honeymooning couple. Of
this fact, Ted was totally ignorant. During the
honeymoon time, Ted journeyed townwards and
pulled up as usual at Tommy’s, taking the horses
out of his buggy and entering the house and bed-
room as he was accustomed to. Not wishing to
disturb Tommy, he refrained from striking a
light, disrobed in the dark, and-—well, you can ’
fill in the rest of the story to suit yourself.

Ballade of Beggars.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The benevolent - asylums and destitute homes are
crowded, and matjy bid people, whose only crime is
poverty, are weekly sent to the gaols to herd with crimi-
nals, that they may not die in the streets.— Melbourne
Daily Paper.
Gentles, we pray you of your charity
That ye give ear to this our piteous case,
For neither food, nor wherewithal have we
To clothe this flesh that here we do abase.
Chill after chill through all our bodies chase ;
And these our features that were once so fair,
Now shrunken are and nipt in winter air.
Here bowed we stand as men who are forlorn.
Let none revile us, no one mock our care—
To this may come all men of women born.
Rich men that in fine chariots happily
Do vie with Father Time his rapid pace,
From out thy fulness, e’en though scantily,
Give crumbs that may put in us heart of grace.
Turn thou our footsteps from this sorry place ;
Much dearth have we that pray this humble
prayer,
Much dearth of hope, much fulness of despair.
Full well we know the biting jibe, the scorn
Of upstart Pride—Oh, fellow man, beware !
To this may come all men of women born.
Why is it crime that these eyes cannot see?
That ploughed and furrowed is this wrinkled
face ?
That after many years spent righteously
Come hard affliction, sorrow, and disgrace?
Send us not into prisons ’mong the base,
‘Mid rogues and murderers to perish there.
Look on these palsied limbs, this whitened hair,
Mark thou these shrinking forms by anguish
worn-
Out of thy human heart some pity share,
To this may come all men of women born.
Croesus, who doth ’gainst future woe prepare
With heaped gold, thy gems and jewels rare
Shall naught console thee when thy heart is torn ?
Feed thou the hungry, clothe the beggar bare—
To this may come all men of women born.
R. H. C.
To a Native Bear.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Grim as the goblin of some German story—
How weird and grey
Thou look’st, up there, against the face of day I
Thou wert not wont to love the morning's glory ;
Toy greeting to the sun’s not con amore.
Dismiss that comic look of apprehension
From off thy phiz ;
A queer, uncanny countenance it is ;
Dread not the sequel of my brief perpension J
There is no ill to thee in my intention.
What! still to me thy dubious gaze directed ?
Take heart of grace!
I love thee for thy philosophic face !
Fear not, I say ! —against thy ribs respected
No lead from this dark tube shall be projected.
Strange are the fancies that thou dost awaken 1
Perhaps, in thee,
Be-inoarnation of some sage we see ;
Thou might’st for any of the tribe be taken.
Shoot thee?— shoot Plato, Socrates, or Bacon !
And yet, no doubt, for all thy reverend seeming,
Thou’st hopes and fears,
And cares, and quarrels, and marsupial dears.
Perchance, e’en now, thy rivals thou’rt blaspheming ;
Perchance not far from thee thy “love lies dreaming.”
Perchance thou’rt wedded, and dost fear a sermon
From anxious mate,
For lingering thus unconscionably late;
Perchance—thy looks suggest the wig and ermine—
Thou hast some complex question to determine.
Perchance—but hold ! a truce to these “perchances 1”
The sun grows high ;
Thou hast a snug cubiculum close by—
If right I read thy surreptitious glances ;
A chambre-A-coucher, the phrase in France is.
Come ! get thee to it, and my Muse befriend
(’Tis sometimes hard to frame a neat conclusion),
By leaving her to make a timely end
Of this effusion!
ALEX. MONTGOMERY.
A lot of old New Zealanders, now residing
in Sydney, with a poetic imagination which would
do credit to a first-class brickbat—not to speak of
the Banjo, or any of his kidney—have discovered
a spot in our midst which they say can knock
spots out of Rotomahana or any other of the hot
lakes in Maoriland. It is—and the information is
certainly well worth knowing by the general
public— not situated in the Blue Mountains, at the
head of the Murray, or far away in the West; but
in prosy Pitt-st., Sydney, close to the Haymarket.
There, rejoicing in the euphonious cognomen of the
Natatorium, they find that, spite of winter winds,
they can luxuriate daily in the soft pleasures of a
swim in a bath (to which that of the famous
Hinemoa is as the family wash-tub to the trans-
lucent depths of the briny blue) whose waters are
for ever invitingly warm. The proprietors of
this unique establishment have indeed solved a
problem, which should gain them not only the
thanks of a grateful community, but also secure to
them an endless procession of would-be bathers
through its doors. By means, which need not be
described here, the water of the baths at The
Natatorium is always at? a temperature 0f,95°, and
persons who hitherto have been unable to get their
daily dip at this, season, owing to the inclement
weather, can now bathe in comfort throughout
the year. The very novelty of swimming in warm
sea water is'sufficient in itself to draw a crowd.*

Warrenheip Murphy, M.P., declares that
Ballarat is the “Oxford of learning” to Australia,
and must therefore become the Federal Capital.
What’s wrong with Jamberoo ?
At Bendigo (Vic.), the other day, a man
who had seduced a one-eyed woman and was
living with her, gave way to irritation, and banged
her on the'head with a stone. Ho knocked out
her other eye, leaving her totally blind, and the
law rose to the occasion and gave him one year—
we mean one month. The person who got the one
year was an old man with “no visible means of
support.” Any individual who wants a month’s
board and lodging to see him through the cold
weather will please take the hint, and knock out
the* eye of a Bendigo magistrate.
“The monetary crisis which has existed
in Portugal for some months past, and which still
continues unabated despite the efforts made to
remedy it, is seriously imperilling the monarchy.
It is seriously feared that a revolution will shortly
take place.—(Age cablegram.)
Mabel Collins, who for a year was Madame
Blavatsky’s colleague in the editorship of Lucifer,
thus writes of her late chief
And yet Ido not regret having known her. She was so
extraordinary a woman. She taught me one great lesson :
1 learned from her how foolish, how “gullible,” how
easily flattered human beings are, taken en masse. Her
contempt for her kind was on the same gigantic scale as
everything else about her, except her marvellously deli-'
cate taper fingers. In all else she was a big woman ; she
had a greater capacity for making black appear white, a
larger waist, a more voracious appetite, a more confirmed
passion for tobacco, a more ceaseless and insatiable hatred
of those whom she thought to be her enemies, a greater
disrespect for les convenances, a worse temper, a greater
command of bad language, and a greater contempt for
the intelligence of her fellow-beings, than I had ever sup-
posed possible to be contained in one person. These, I
suppose, must be reckoned as her vices, though whether
a creature so indifferent to all ordinary standards of right
and wrong can be held to have virtues or vices I know
not. . . . I commenced by being interested in her pro-
fessed knowledge of magic, and very curious about her
greater pretensions. I ended by arriving at a condition of
wonder at her genius in charlatanry, her sublime confi-
dence in herself, her contempt for everyone else. I
sometimes think the intensity of this contempt confused
people and made them foolish. I have seen her, by the
simplest sleight of hand, produce new American stamps,
and fresh sovereigns, and clean bank-notes, the newness
about them being a proof of her just having created them
or drawn them out of the vastness of the infinite being -
to the positive conviction of apparently intelligent wit-
nesses. I have known her profess to read sealed letters
by the help of her psychic powers, and make shrewd
business men think she had done so, when a mere child,
not under her spell, would have pointed out that these
same letters and a hot-water jug had had ample oppor-
tunity for making each other's acquaintance, with her
kind assistance. I have known her intimidate a whole
gathering of (at other times) sensible persons by the sim-
ple announcement that she could see her “master”'
standing among them, and that he was very angry.
I have heard from different sources that some
very strange revelation about Madame Blavatsky
would bo heard after her death. There are persons iii
different countries in possession of documents and letters
which are to be published in due course, so it has
always been said. I hope this may be so. Her own
letters, if collected and printed, would be curious; and
would bo a valuable lesson to those who are likely to b
fascinated or flattered by an intellectual syren. Not that
another so great is likely to arise among us in this
century. Many, however, will be interested to know,
once for all, whether this mystic individual was an
adventuress pure and simple, or whether she was, as
some believe, a political spy* or agent, who used the cloak
of a religious movement to cover her actions, and made
a set of fanatics the unconscious 1 arbourers of a few
political intriguers in any centre she lived in. It would
lie impossible for anyone who knew h:r for so short a
time as I did to form any opinion as to this. Whatevei
the vital motive of her charlatanry was, it was marvel
lously audacious and entirely unscrupulous. A romantic'
and infamous career is closed, and now that she is dead,
we who have lost any of our illusions with her, can only
say, “ Peace be to her soul.”
This all strengthens the original impression (it
grew as days went on) which we formed on meet-
ing our late visitor, Colonel Olcott—that he was
a softy. It is on record that Blavatsky used to
bully him in public.
Spurgeon tells a good one. A reverend
gentleman in East London was remonstrated with
for not converting the thieves and harlots of his
parish. “Ah,” said he, “my congregation would
be too poor to support them !”
“ Rev. H. L. Jackson considered that r
clergyman should take his part in the doings of
the world, and he saw no harm in visiting the
theatre. . . . Rev. A. R. Bartlett was with
Mr. Jackson, and he explained that on the steamer
which brought him from England were some
good actors, the brightest and most helpful per-
sons on board ship. At the Sunday services more
attentive and reverent listeners could not be
found.” They were good actors, perhaps.
Here—we quote from the Manchester
(Eng.) Sunday Chronicle—are The Bulletin’s
sentiments, though we perhaps have never suc-
ceeded in putting the case so neatly ;
The Chronicle cannot be more correctly called a labour
organ than a sporting organ, or a Socialist organ, or a
publicans’ organ, or a party organ. Our paper is not
bound to the chariot-wheels of any person or party, and
we object to any label whatever. We advocate Socialism,
though I believe our staff are mainly strong Indi-
vidualists ; for we consider Socialism would prove most
advantageous to the rank and file of humanity, and sink
our own preference for the general good. You will
invariably find the Chronicle on the weaker side, and so
we are on the side of the labourer. But if the labourer
had the courage to use his electoral power, as he could
and should, for his own advantage, we should most likely
then be found pleading the cause of down-trodden dukes,
and agitating for the amelioration of the lot of miserable
marquises and blighted baronets.
An English physician writes: “I draw a
distinction between inaccuracy and untruth.
Women are often inaccurate because they are
emotional. They describe sensations rather than
relate facts, but this is a constitutional, not a
moral, fault.” If, say, Mrs. Crawford didn't
quite tell the truth in the Dilke case, her fault
was not a moral but a constitutional defect. In
other words, she was constitutionally immoral.
And so in a recent memorable breach-of-promise
case at Sydney ; and if in the Gordon-Cumming
case, any one of the lady-witnesses didn’t stick to
solemn fact, we must excuse her on the ground of
the defect in her constitution. Woman as a liar has
wonderful privileges. She is more readily
believed, especially when she is at all good-
looking, than man, and her little perjuries when
detected are written down as constitutional
defects which the man whom she sends to gaol
must pity, inasmuch as they cannot fairly be
resented.
GOOD OLD CRISIS!
The light is coming by degrees;
Now money with the Portuguese
At such a price is
That European monarchs fear
Another crown may disappear.
0, good old Crisis !
It was but yesterday Brazil
Declined to fill Don Pedro’s till :
He couldn’t ’tico his
Beloved subjects from revolt;
The sodgers turned, lie had to bolt.
0, good old Crisis !
Queen V. upon her English throne
Sits sad at heart, which cold as stone,
Or e’en as ice, is :
Her brow is black, her temper vext,
She wonders who’ll be going next.
0, good old Crisis !
The Czar reflects, with visage white,
How one attacked by dynamite
Gone in a trice is :
Young Wilhelm is uneasy, too —
What might the German army do
In good old Crisis ?
The pert and puny King of Spain’s
No doubt employed in “ playing trains ”
His taffy nice is ;
To purple born, a baby king,
He doesn’t dream, poor, ickle sing !
Of good old Crisis !
King Otto’s off his head, they say,
And peeling ’takers all the day
His only vice is ;
With politics quite out of touch,
He wouldn’t be affected much
By good old Crisis.
But nowadays, ’twill be allowed,
The outlook for the royal crowd
(To be precise) is
Dam bad; the nations of the earth
Are not impressed with royal worth—
O, good old Crisis !
A time will come (as in the play
The heavy villain likes to say),
And onr advice is—
Just wait a bit, content to know
That all the lot are bound to go.
O, good old Crisis !

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors I Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
tor all scouring and house-cleaning.

Two Men Wrong—One Man Right.
“ You will he in kingdom-come in less than twelve
months 1 ”
“ You can’t live three years ! ” •
“ You are not going to die ! ”
These three prophecies were addressed to the
same man. The last one had the most comfort in
it, yet he couldn’t tell which had the most inspira-
tion.
Here’s the story. It isn’t pleasant reading at
first, but it “ comes out ” all right, as the children
say.
Perhaps it’s better to let our friend tell it him-
self. We all like the pronoun “I,” that’s why
an autobiography is never dull. This relator is a
butcher of Auckland, N. Z., and Ids style is so
clear and good, it needs no editing.
He says : “ Some five years ago, when lifting a
quarter of beef, I noticed a remarkable soreness in
the small of my back. I called a doctor, gave up
business, and took to my bed. He prescribed a
lotion to be rubbed in around the region of the
kidneys. This was done, and I remained in bed
several days, suffering exerntiating agony. At
last, having examined the kidney secretions, the
doctor-said, ‘You have Bright’s disease, and will
he in kingdom-come in less than twelve months.’
“Never believing I had Bright’s disease, I
thought, however, it would be as well to insmo
my life. On examination, the company’s physician
refused to pass me for life insurance, saying,
‘Yon can't live three years.’
“ About three months afterwards I was again
seized with a severe attack, and went about more
dead than alive. Finally I broke down and again
took to my bed, as was supposed, for the last
time. I sold one of my businesses so as not to
leave too many affairs for my wife to snnervise, 1
made my will, and explained to my wife what to
do in case of my death. I can well remember her
listening vith tears in her eyes to what she be-
lieved were my last instructions. I then lingered
for some weeks, waiting for death to free me from
my sufferings—taking whatever medicines were
prescribed, but deriving little or no relief.
“One evening a friend called, and wo talked
over my case and the very serious turn it had
taken. Presently he said, ‘ Yon aro not going to
die. Have you tried Seigei’s Syrup yet ? ’
“I confessed that I had not. In fact, I had
religiously followed the instructions of my medical
advisers. My friend persuaded me to try Seigel's
Syrup, and I began at once, taking twenty-drop
doses according to the directions. In about a
week I felt a little more life in me, and after
having finished the second bottle I was convinced
that improvement had fairly set in. I continued
taking the Syrup until I had consumed ten bottles,
then went downstairs to work again, still using
the Syrup. After having taken from eighteen to
twenty bottles altogether, I found myself entirely
well. It is now four years since I took my last
dose of Mother Seigel’s Syrup for Bright’s disease
of the kidneys, arid I have suffered no symptoms
of kidney complaint since.
“(Signed) Robert Hutchinson, Butcher.
Wellington-street, Auckland, New Zealand.”
We print Mr. Hutchinson’s statement as ho
wrote it, yet it is not probable that his malady
was actually Bright’s disease, as that is a degenera-
tion or destruction of the substance of the kidneys, j
and difficult, if not impossible, to cure. What he
really suffered from was, no doubt, a debilitated
state of the system, originating in indigestion and
dyspepsia, with severe kidney symptoms, as is often
the case. When we remember that nearly all
maladies, including rheumatism, gout, consump-
tion, liver complaint, heart disease, &c., are due
to poisons in the blocd, arising from fermented
food in the digestive tract, we can see why Mother
Seigel’s Syrup is victorious over so great a variety
of apparently diverse diseases. And because of
the one source of these complaints, serious and
often fatal mistakes are made in the diagnosis and
in the treatment. The point to emphasise is this :
Heart disease, consumption, and Bright’s disease
are much rarer than they arc supposed to he.
Generally what seems like them is a group of
symptoms of indigestion and dyspepsia. It is
always best to take the advice of Air. Hutchinson’s
friend when he said, “Yon are not going to die ;
try Seigel's Syrup.”
One big newspaper company up North is
said to be dropping £GOO a month.
The P, and (). Co. are lengthening their
two well-known steamers, the Rome and the
Carthage, and giving them entirely different bow
lines. They expect to be able to drive them at an
average of something like two knots an hour
faster than they have been driven up to the
present time, without any increase in the cost of
fuel.
The counters produced in Court in the
baccarat case were not those belonging to
“ Wales,” but were specially made for the purpose
of the trial. It was hoped at one time, says
I igaro, that the question of the ownership of the
counters would have been avoided. The Prince’s
own counters are stamped with the crown and
feathers. They form part of the furniture of a
case fitted up with the appurtenances for all the
games of the board. The case was a present to
Turn, from Alfred de Rothschild.
It is stated that there is an unrepealed
law of Charles II. still in existence, by which a
man who is hungry and dead-broke can go into
anybody’s premises, and after furnishing reason-
able evidence of his condition can eat as much as
he is able to hold. This old statute wants to be
looked up at once.
The question concerning the origin of the
word “Toowoomba” is thus conclusively settled
by Archibald Meston, of Brisbane. He says:
“Toowoomba” is a word in the dialect of the
“ Gqooeeburra ” or “Fire Blacks” of the
Darling Downs, who spoke “Wakka.” It is
derived from “Toowoom,” the name of a small
native melon which grew prolifically in the
Toowoomba Valley. The terminal “ba” is
equivalent to the adverb “there,” and the whole
word to a Gooneeburra black would mean “ the
place where we g t the Toowoom.” Toowoomba
is one of the few native names spelled exactly as
pronounced ly t ho old blacks and by the sur-
vivors of to-day.

Rev. Brewster, at a recent clerical
conference, thinks that “ Parsons should visit
their people, not for the purposes of going to the
theatre or playing a friendly game of whist.
This is quite out of the line of the work of the
Church, as it is not calculated to raise men
morally.” Yet a good many parsons induce their
flocks every Sunday to indulge in “threepenny
nap. ”
The Broken Hill Proprietary lode (N.S. W.)
is 39G0ft. long and 10,5ft. wide, or a little wider
than Collins-t treet, Melbourne. Taking the depth
at 300ft., its owners have yet rooted out only one-
seventh of the contents of the lode, or 74G tons of
silver.
Barlow’s Pharmacy Act Amendment Bill?
summarily slaughtered by the Queensland
Assembly, the other day, proposed to permit all
pharmaceutists holding certificates of any Aus-
tralian co’ony to practise in Queensland. At pre-
sent, a duly-qualified chemist of Victoria is a
trade pariah in Queensland and must be re-
apprenticed for three years and re-examined
locally. Similarly with N.S.W. and S.A. This
is a condition of unf- derated dry rot, but the lines
of reciprocity are being daily drawn closer,
though the Victorian body of poisoners claim that
their test of examination is higher than that of
any of the sister provinces.
According to Burrum Powers, 3000 copies
of the interdicted Dead Bird were once smuggled
through the post into Queensland, each wrapped
up inside a War Cry. This was neat, but it’s a
question whether the Cry isn’t the more
“blasphemous ” publication of the two.

Dear Bulletin,—The other day you had a

After his return for St. Leonards didn't he go and

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at Sydney, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia's Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Pu'iry.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics,
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin wai
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
arm has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin's inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established .Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress : it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official —that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightast
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

10th December, ISBS.
To H. F. Kugf.lmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last 1 began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in ray left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted yon, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two,pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant,.this 10th.dAy.Qf .Decciqbtr.J^tt.
